دائمًا فنقول كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، ولا شيء من (ب) (أ) دائمًا فلا شيء من (ج) (أ) دائمًا: وإنها تنعكس كنفسها لا شيء من «أ» «ج» دائمًا؛ وقد كان كل «أ» (ج) لا بالضرورة، هذا خلف. وعلى هذا في الثاني منهما فإنك إذا قلت كل «ج» «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، وكل «أ» «ج» بالإمكان العام فإنه ينتج قولنا بعض «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى، أو بالخلف كما مر . ثم الصغرى إذا كانت ضرورية والكبرى ممكنة خاصة كانت أو عامة فالنتيجة مطلقة عامة كما في قولنا كل كاتب إنسان بالضرورة، وكل طبيب كاتب بالإمكان الخاص فبعض الإنسان طبيب بالإطلاق العام. وذلك لأنه ينتج بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى من الشكل الأول موجبة كلية ضرورية؛ وإنها تنعكس مطلقة عامة. وأما الخلف فكما مرَّ ذكره من قبل. ومن زعم تقاعد الممكنات في الأشكال التي مرَّ ذكرها فقد زعم في هذا الشكل كذلك؛ وذلك لأنها من جملة ما لا يصح استعماله فيه أصلا. وكيف والخاصة للنوع إذا حملت على فصل النوع الآخر بالإمكان؛ وحمل ذلك النوع على فصله بالضرورة في الكبرى أو سلب عنه بالضرورة فذلك لا ينتج البتة لا سلبًا ولا إيجابا على ما عرف من قبل. غير أنه من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه بأنه من الوهميات لما أن الثابت بالإمكان عبارة عما إذا فرض ثبوته بالفعل كان ذلك الفرض فرضًا ممكنا؛ وإنه لا يكون كذلك فيما لا يكون منتجا من الأمثلة المشهورة عندهم في هذه الصورة فلا يكون قادحًا فيما ادعيناه وهو صحة استعمال الممكنات في هذا الشكل.
We say always: every J is B necessarily, and nothing of B is A always, so nothing of J is A always. And it converts like itself: nothing of A is J always; but it was that every A is J not necessarily, this is a contradiction. And upon this in the second of them: if you say every J is B with specific possibility, and every A is J with general possibility, then it produces our statement 'some B is A with general possibility' by making the minor the major and the major the minor, or by reduction to absurdity as before. Then if the minor is necessary and the major is possible, whether specific or general, the conclusion is general absolute, as in our statement: every writer is human necessarily, and every physician is writer with specific possibility, so some human is physician with absolute generality. That is because it produces, by making the minor the major and the major the minor from the first figure, a universal affirmative necessary (proposition); and it converts to a general absolute (proposition). As for reduction to absurdity, it is as previously mentioned. And whoever claims that possible (premises) are inefficient in the figures that have been mentioned, claims the same in this figure; that is because they are among what cannot be used in it at all. How so? For the property of a species when predicated of the differentia of another species with possibility, and that species is predicated of its differentia necessarily in the major or negated from it necessarily, that produces nothing at all, neither negation nor affirmation, as known before. Nevertheless, it is among what has been discussed before, that it is from illusory (arguments) because that which is established as possible is an expression of something which, if its actual existence is assumed, that assumption is a possible assumption; and it is not so in the non-productive examples famous among them in this form, so it does not refute what we have claimed, namely the soundness of using possible (premises) in this figure.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the second figure's syllogisms with modal premises. The author first states a rule: if we have 'every J is B necessarily' and 'nothing of B is A always', then 'nothing of J is A always' follows. This conclusion can be converted (by simple conversion of universal negative) to 'nothing of A is J always', but the assumption that 'every A is J not necessarily' contradicts the first premise, leading to absurdity. Next, the author explains that when the minor is possible-specific and the major is possible-general, the conclusion 'some B is A with general possibility' can be obtained by swapping the premises (reducing to first figure) or by reductio ad absurdum. The core rule is: if the minor is necessary and the major is possible (either specific or general), the conclusion is general absolute. This is illustrated with an example: every writer is necessarily human, and every physician is possibly a writer, therefore some human is absolutely a physician. The proof: by converting the premises (making the original minor the major and the original major the minor) we get a first-figure universal affirmative necessary syllogism, which converts to a general absolute particular. The author then addresses an objection: someone might claim that possible premises are inefficient in this figure, citing examples where combining a possible property of a species with a necessary predication of the species' differentia yields no conclusion. The author counters that such counterexamples are illusory, because the possible proposition implies that assuming actuality leads to a valid actual conclusion, and the famous non-productive examples do not meet this condition. Thus they do not refute the general validity of using possible premises in the second figure.

بالإمكان الخاص
بالإمكان العام

When a necessary premise meets a possible premise in the second figure, the conclusion is absolute, not modal.

Defending the Use of Possible Premises in the Second Figure
Author refutes objection that possible premises are invalid in second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate an objection against using possible premises in the second figure and explain the author's response using the concept of 'illusory counterexamples'.

Core Idea
Apparent counterexamples to possible-premise syllogisms are illusory because they violate the condition of actual possibility.

Some logicians claim that possible premises are inefficient in the second figure, citing examples where a possible property of a species combined with a necessary predication of a differentia fails to produce a conclusion. The author argues that these are 'illusory' (وهَمِيَّات) because the possible premise's actualization does not hold in those cases. Let's dissect this defense.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the claim: 'من زعم تقاعد الممكنات في الأشكال... فقد زعم في هذا الشكل كذلك' – someone says possible premises are useless in this figure. The counterexample: a species' property predicated of another species' differentia with possibility, and that species predicated of its own differentia necessarily, yields no conclusion.
Example in modern terms: 'Every differentia of species A is possibly property P; every species A is necessarily differentia X.' No conclusion follows.
2
Understand the author's rebuttal: 'illusory'
The author says these counterexamples are from 'الوهميات' (imaginary/illusory). The reason: a proposition with possibility means that if we assume the predicate actually holds of the subject, that assumption is possible. In their counterexample, this assumption leads to an impossibility or invalidity, so the example is not a genuine counterexample.
The author: 'الثابت بالإمكان عبارة عما إذا فرض ثبوته بالفعل كان ذلك الفرض فرضًا ممكنا؛ وإنه لا يكون كذلك فيما لا يكون منتجا.'
3
Apply the condition to the counterexample
Take the counterexample: suppose 'Every X is Y possibly' means that 'some X is Y' is possible. If we actualize that possibility, we get 'some X is Y actually'. If that actualized premise, combined with the necessary premise, still yields no conclusion, then the original possible premise was not truly possible – the assumption of actuality was impossible. Thus the counterexample is invalid.
This is the key: possible premises must be genuine possibilities. The counterexample involves an impossible actualization.
4
Conclude: possible premises are valid in second figure
Since the objection relies on illusory examples, the author's claim that possible premises are sound in the second figure stands. The examples cited by opponents do not meet the definition of genuine possibility.
The author ends with: 'فلا يكون قادحًا فيما ادعيناه وهو صحة استعمال الممكنات في هذا الشكل.'

Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure: Universal Negative Conclusions
Author details modal syllogisms in the second figure that yield universal negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate modal syllogisms in the second figure that yield universal negative conclusions, identifying the required modal conditions and applying proof methods.

Core Idea
The second figure yields universal negatives when the minor is a convertible universal negative and the major is a universal affirmative.

In the previous division, we saw syllogisms that produce universal negative conclusions in the second figure. Now the author takes us deeper, exploring how the modality (necessary, perpetual, conditional) of the premises affects the modality of the conclusion. This is crucial for theological arguments where modal strength matters: a necessary conclusion requires necessary premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the minor premise (usually the one with the middle term as subject) and the major premise (with the middle term as predicate). The conclusion in this division is always universal negative.
In the example, minor: 'nothing of J is B necessarily', major: 'every A is J necessarily', conclusion: 'nothing of B is A necessarily'.
2
Determine the modal status of each premise
Check whether each premise is necessary, perpetual, conditional, etc. The author details which modal combinations yield a valid conclusion and which do not.
For instance, if the minor is necessary and the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. If the minor is perpetual but the major is possible, no conclusion follows.
3
Apply the proof method
The author provides two ways: either by transposition (swap premises and convert the conclusion) or by reductio ad absurdum. Follow the reductio: assume the contradictory (a particular affirmative with possibility), combine with the major, derive a contradiction with the minor.
The reductio is more general; practice it for different modal combinations.
4
Check validity for modal variations
For each combination mentioned (e.g., minor generally conditional, major perpetual), verify whether the conclusion follows and whether the modal matches the rule given.
When the minor is specific conventional, the conclusion is general with the same condition as the major, etc.

And the second of the divisions concerning what produces the universal negative, like when the minor is from the convertible universal negatives and the major from the universal affirmatives. Then if the minor is necessary, the conclusion is necessary, as it is said: necessarily nothing of J is B, and necessarily every A is J, so necessarily nothing of B is A. The demonstration of it is either by making the minor the major and the major the minor then converting the conclusion, or by reduction to absurdity and others. That is because if our statement 'necessarily nothing of B is A' is not true, its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'some B is A with general possibility'. Then we combine it with the major and say: some B is A with general possibility, and every A is J necessarily, so some B is J necessarily. Then it follows that some J is B with general absolute; but it was that nothing of J is B necessarily, this is a contradiction. And upon this, if the minor is perpetual, then the conclusion is perpetual by the two mentioned ways. And if the minor is generally conditional and the major is from the affirmatives whose negatives are convertible, like the perpetual, general, conditional, and conventional, then the conclusion is like the major as before. So it is said, for example: necessarily nothing of J is B as long as J, and every A is J always, so nothing of B is A always. And if the major is from the specific ones, the conclusion is general like the major, as it is said: necessarily nothing of J is B as long as J, and necessarily every A is J not always but as long as A, so necessarily nothing of B is A as long as B. And upon this if the major is specifically conventional. And if the minor is generally conventional and the major is from the perpetuals, the conclusion is like the major as before, contrary to if the major is from the conditionals and conventionals; for then the conclusion is like the minor; and it does not produce with other than the affirmatives. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the second division of the second figure, which produces universal negative conclusions. The author specifies that this occurs when the minor premise is a convertible universal negative and the major is a universal affirmative. The first subcase: if the minor is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. For example, 'necessarily nothing of J is B' and 'necessarily every A is J' yield 'necessarily nothing of B is A'. The proof is given via two methods: either by swapping the premises (making the minor the major and the major the minor) and then converting the conclusion of the resulting first-figure syllogism, or by reductio ad absurdum. In the reductio, assume the negation of the conclusion ('some B is A with general possibility'), combine it with the major ('every A is J necessarily') to get 'some B is J necessarily', which by conversion gives 'some J is B with general absolute', contradicting the original minor ('nothing of J is B necessarily'). The same holds if the minor is perpetual (dā'ima), yielding a perpetual conclusion. However, if the minor is perpetual but the major is one of the two possible (general or specific), no conclusion follows because the major term, though always denied of the middle, may not be denied of the minor; the middle does not necessarily contain the minor. The author then discusses various modal combinations for the premises: if the minor is generally conditional (sharṭiyya ʿāmma) and the major is an affirmative whose contrapositive is convertible (like perpetual, general, conditional, or conventional), the conclusion is like the major in modality. Examples are given: 'necessarily nothing of J is B as long as J' and 'every A is J always' yield 'nothing of B is A always'. If the major is specific, the conclusion is general with the same duration as the major. If the minor is conventionally general and the major perpetual, the conclusion follows the major, but if the major is conditional or conventional, the conclusion follows the minor. The text cuts off while discussing the case where the minor is specific and the major is from the convertible affirmatives.

Key Terms
سالبة كلية
دائمة
مشروطة عامة
عرفية خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Minor: perpetual universal negative; Major: generally possible universal affirmative.
C) Minor: generally conditional universal negative; Major: perpetual universal affirmative.
D) Minor: specifically conventional universal negative; Major: generally conventional universal affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Modal syllogisms in the second figure require careful matching of modal strength; a necessary conclusion demands necessary premises, but some combinations (e.g., perpetual minor with possible major) yield no conclusion.

The Syllogism

Modal Syllogism: Necessary Minor Yields Necessary Conclusion
Author demonstrates that a necessary minor premise yields a necessary conclusion in modal syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that a necessary minor premise in a categorical syllogism yields a necessary conclusion using both the conversion method and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The modality of the minor premise determines the modality of the conclusion.

In modal logic, the strength of the premises affects the strength of the conclusion. This passage demonstrates that when the minor premise is necessary, the conclusion must also be necessary. The author provides an example in the first figure and offers two rigorous proofs: one by swapping premises and converting, and another by reductio. This lesson will walk you through these proofs so you can apply them to any modal syllogism in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal syllogism and its terms
Find the three terms used: the middle term (ج), the subject of the conclusion (ب), and the predicate of the conclusion (أ). Note the modality 'necessarily' (بالضرورة) on both premises and conclusion.
In this example, the conclusion is 'no B is A' (equivalent to 'no A is B').
2
Understand the main claim
The author asserts that if the minor premise (صغرى) is necessary, then the conclusion (نتيجة) is also necessary. This is not automatic; it requires proof because the major premise might be non-necessary.
Here both premises are necessary, but the claim is about the minor alone.
3
Analyze the first proof: conversion method
Swap the premises: treat the original minor as new major and original major as new minor. Then convert the conclusion of the new syllogism to obtain the original conclusion. This shows that the original conclusion follows necessarily.
This method relies on the convertibility of universal negative propositions.
4
Analyze the second proof: reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion (i.e., 'some B is A with general possibility'). Combine with the major premise to derive 'some B is J necessarily'. Convert to 'some J is B generally', which contradicts the original minor premise 'no J is B necessarily'.
The contradiction proves the original conclusion must be true.
5
Extend to perpetual modality
The author notes that the same two methods work when the minor premise is perpetual (دائمة) rather than necessary. This suggests that the rule applies to any strong modality of the minor.
Check if your text distinguishes necessary and perpetual; here they are treated similarly for the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الصغرى إذا كانت ضرورية كانت النتيجة ضرورية كما يقال بالضرورة لا شيء من (ج) (ب)، وبالضرورة كل «أ» «ج» فبالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (أ). والبيان فيه إما بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى ضغرى ثم عكس النتيجة، وإما بالخلف وغيره وذلك لأنه إذا لم يصدق قولنا فبالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (أ) صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول بعض «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام، وكل (أ) «ج» بالضرورة فبعض «ب» «ج» بالضرورة، وحينئذ يلزم قولنا بعض (ج) (ب) بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان لا شيء من «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، هذا خلف. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة؛ إذ النتيجة دائمة بالطريقين المذكورين.
Then, when the minor premise is necessary, the conclusion is necessary, as it is said: necessarily, no J is B; and necessarily, every A is J; therefore necessarily, no B is A. The demonstration of that is either by making the minor major and the major minor then converting the conclusion, or by reductio and other methods. That is because if our statement 'necessarily, no B is A' is not true, then its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'some B is A with general possibility'. So we combine it with the major and say: some B is A with general possibility, and every A is J necessarily, so some B is J necessarily. Then it follows that some J is B with general absoluteness, while it was the case that no J is B necessarily. This is a contradiction. And similarly, when the minor is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual by the two mentioned methods.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a modal syllogistic rule. The author states that when the minor premise is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. He provides an example with terms J, B, A: 'Necessarily, no J is B; necessarily, every A is J; therefore necessarily, no B is A.' This is a valid syllogism in the first figure (Celarent with modal qualifiers). The author then offers two methods to prove this rule. The first method involves swapping the premises: make the minor 'no J is B' the new major, and the major 'every A is J' the new minor, then convert the conclusion to get the original conclusion. This reduction to a first-figure syllogism shows validity. The second method is reductio ad absurdum: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, i.e., 'some B is A with general possibility', combine it with the major to derive 'some B is J necessarily', which by conversion gives 'some J is B generally', contradicting the original minor premise 'no J is B necessarily'. The author concludes that the same holds when the minor is perpetual (دائمة) instead of necessary, meaning the conclusion is perpetual. This passage forms the foundation for modal syllogistic reasoning in the text.

Key Terms
ضرورية (ḍarūriyya): A proposition that is necessarily true, i.e., its truth is not subject to temporal or conditional restriction. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
دائمة (dā'ima): A proposition that is always true (perpetual), without temporal exception. [Note: Often synonymous with ضرورية in some contexts, but here distinguished as a separate modality.]
صغرى (ṣughrā): The minor premise of a syllogism; the premise containing the minor term (subject of the conclusion). [Note: Standard throughout Islamic logic.]
كبرى (kubrā): The major premise of a syllogism; the premise containing the major term (predicate of the conclusion). [Note: Standard throughout Islamic logic.]
نتيجة (natīja): The conclusion of a syllogism, which follows necessarily from the premises. [Note: Standard.]
عكس (ʿaks): Conversion of a proposition by swapping subject and predicate; here used to transform the conclusion after swapping premises. [Note: Standard.]
خلف (khalf): Reductio ad absurdum; proving a statement by showing that its falsehood leads to a contradiction. [Note: Standard.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): The contradictory of a proposition; truth of one implies falsehood of the other. [Note: Standard.]
إمكان عام (imkān ʿāmm): General possibility; a modal term meaning 'possible at some time' without specification. [Note: Contrasted with 'imkān khāṣṣ' (specific possibility) in later texts.]
إطلاق عام (iṭlāq ʿāmm): General absoluteness; an unqualified proposition without temporal or modal restriction. [Note: Standard.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) possible
B) necessary
C) perpetual
D) contingent

Key Takeaway
In modal categorical syllogisms, a necessary minor premise necessitates a necessary conclusion, provable by conversion or reductio.

Why a Perpetual Minor with a Possible Major Produces No Conclusion
Author identifies a non-productive modal combination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a modal syllogism fails due to a mismatch between a perpetual minor and a possible major, and explain why no conclusion follows.

Core Idea
A perpetual minor and a possible major yield no conclusion: the middle term does not guarantee containment.

In modal logic, not every combination of modal premises produces a valid conclusion. The author examines a specific case where the minor premise is perpetual and the major premise is from the possible (contingent) propositions. Understanding why this combination fails is crucial for correctly assessing the validity of modal arguments in Islamic sciences, especially when building proofs in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Set up the syllogism
Write the minor as a perpetual universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B perpetually') and the major as a possible universal negative (e.g., 'No B is C possibly'). Note the modal qualities.
The minor is perpetual, meaning always true for all times the subject exists. The major is possible, meaning the negation is not necessary.
2
Check the containment condition
The author argues that the middle term (B) does not encompass the minor term (A) in a way that transfers the denial. Since the major is only possible, even though A is perpetually B, we cannot be sure that the denial of C from B applies to A.
In a valid first-figure syllogism, the middle term must be contained in the minor or contain it; here, the possible major breaks the necessary transfer.
3
Observe the non-productivity
Conclude that no categorical relationship between A and C can be deduced. The modalities prevent a determinate conclusion.
This block demonstrates that modal alignment is essential: a necessary (perpetual) minor cannot combine with a non-necessary major to yield a categorical result.
4
Contrast with productive cases
Compare with cases where both premises are perpetual or where the major is also necessary. The author later shows that when the major is perpetual or generally conditioned, a conclusion does follow.
This contrast reinforces the principle that both premises must have compatible modal strengths for the syllogism to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة، والكبرى من الممكنتين فذلك لا ينتج لما أن الأكبر وإن كان مسلوبًا عن الأوسط دائما فلا يدرى بأنه مسلوب عن الأصغر أم لا؛ والأوسط لا يكون متضمنا للأصغر وإن كان يمكن أنه في ضمنه. وإن كانت الصغرى مشروطة عامة والكبرى من الموجبات المنعكسة سوالبها نحو الدائمتين والعامتين والمشروطتين والعرفيتين فالنتيجة كالكبرى لما مر. فيقال مثلا بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» ما دام ج، وكل «أ» «ج» دائمًا فلا شيء من ب أ دائما. وإن كانت الكبرى من الخاصتين فالنتيجة عامة الكبرى كما يقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، وبالضرورة كل «أ» «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ» فبالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ما دام (ب). وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى عرفية خاصة. وإن كانت الصغرى عرفية عامة والكبرى من الدائمتين فالنتيجة كالكبرى لما مر على خلاف ما إذا كانت الكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين؛ إذ النتيجة كالصغرى حينئذ؛ ولا ينتج مع الغير من الموجبات. وإن كانت الصغرى من الخاصتين، والكبرى من الموجبات المنعكسة سوالبها سوى الدائمتين فالنتيجة كعكس الكبرى. فيقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل ما دام (ج)، وكل «أ» (ج) ما دام (أ) فلا شيء من «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى عرفية خاصة ولا ينتج مع الغير من الموجبات كذلك كما لا ينتج مع الدائمتين.
As for when the minor is perpetual and the major is from among the possible [propositions], then it does not produce [a conclusion] because although the major term is denied of the middle term perpetually, it is not known whether it is denied of the minor term or not; and the middle term does not encompass the minor term even though it is possible that it is encompassed by it. And if the minor is generally conditioned and the major is from among the affirmative [premises] whose negatives are convertible, like the perpetual, the general, the conditioned, and the conventional, then the conclusion is like the major for the reason mentioned. So for example it is said: necessarily, no J is B as long as J; and every A is J perpetually; therefore perpetually, no B is A perpetually. And if the major is from the two special [propositions], then the conclusion is universal like the major, as it is said: necessarily, no J is B as long as J; and necessarily, every A is J not perpetually but as long as A; therefore necessarily, no B is A as long as B. And similarly if the major is conventional special. And if the minor is conventional general and the major is from the two perpetual [propositions], then the conclusion is like the major for the reason mentioned, contrary to when the major is from the two conditioned and the two conventional; for then the conclusion is like the minor; and it does not produce [a conclusion] with other affirmative [premises]. And if the minor is from the two special [propositions] and the major is from the affirmative [premises] whose negatives are convertible except the two perpetual, then the conclusion is like the converse of the major. So it is said: necessarily, no J is B not perpetually but as long as J; and every A is J as long as A; therefore no B is A with general absoluteness for the reason mentioned. And similarly if the major is conventional special, and it does not produce [a conclusion] with other affirmative [premises] just as it does not produce with the two perpetual.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of various combinations of modal premises in a syllogism and their outcomes. The author first considers the case where the minor is perpetual (دائمة) and the major is from the possible propositions (ممكنتين). He states that no conclusion follows because although the major term is perpetually denied of the middle term, we cannot be sure it is denied of the minor term due to the lack of containment. Next, he discusses cases where the minor is generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة) and the major belongs to a set of affirmative premises whose negatives are convertible (i.e., the major is convertible when negated). In such cases, the conclusion has the same modality as the major. An example is given: a necessary universal negative major with a temporal condition ('as long as J') and a perpetual universal affirmative minor yield a perpetual universal negative conclusion. Then, if the major is from the two special propositions (خاصتين), the conclusion is universal like the major, illustrated with a temporal negative minor and a temporally limited affirmative minor producing a temporally limited negative conclusion. The author notes the same for conventional special major. Then he examines the case where the minor is conventional general (عرفية عامة) and the major is perpetual: the conclusion is like the major, but if the major is conditioned or conventional, the conclusion is like the minor. No conclusion is produced with other affirmatives. Finally, if the minor is from the two special and the major is from convertible affirmatives except the two perpetual, the conclusion is like the converse of the major. An example is given: a temporally limited negative and a temporally limited affirmative yield an absolute negative conclusion. The author emphasizes that many combinations do not yield a conclusion, highlighting the complexity of modal logic.

ممكنتين

A syllogism with a perpetual minor and a possible major fails because the middle term does not guarantee containment of the minor, so no conclusion follows.

When the Conclusion Follows the Major: Minor Generally Conditioned
Author states rule and gives example for a productive modal combination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict that when the minor is generally conditioned and the major belongs to a class of convertible affirmatives, the conclusion matches the modality of the major.

Core Idea
A generally conditioned minor paired with a convertible major yields a conclusion identical to the major in modality.

The author now turns to productive combinations. When the minor is generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة) and the major is from the set of affirmative propositions whose negatives are convertible (including perpetual, general, conditioned, and conventional propositions), the conclusion has the same modality as the major. This rule streamlines the evaluation of many modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor is a generally conditioned proposition (e.g., 'Every A is B as long as A exists'). The major is from the special set: universal negatives that are convertible, like perpetual, general, conditioned, or conventional universal negatives.
The key is that the major's negative is convertible, meaning its converse is also true. This property is essential for the conclusion to mirror the major.
2
Follow the example
The author gives an example: minor 'Every A is J perpetually', major 'Necessarily, no J is B as long as J'. The conclusion is 'Perpetually, no B is A perpetually' – the modality of the major (perpetual negative) is preserved.
Notice the conclusion is a perpetual universal negative, just like the major. The minor's temporal condition 'as long as J' disappears because the perpetual minor overrides it.
3
Generalize the pattern
Whenever the minor is generally conditioned and the major is from that convertible set, the conclusion has the same modality and quantity as the major. The minor's condition does not affect the conclusion's modality.
This is because the major's modality is stronger or equal in strength, and the convertible property allows the inference to hold.
4
Contrast with non-convertible majors
The author later notes that if the major is not from this set (e.g., from the two special propositions), the conclusion still follows the major but with the minor's condition appearing in the conclusion. This refines the rule.
Understanding this contrast is crucial for correctly applying the rule in varied syllogisms.

The Syllogism

When the Conclusion Follows the Minor or Fails: Minor Conventional General
Author gives rule for minor conventional general with various majors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the conclusion follows the minor or fails when the minor is conventional general and the major varies.

Core Idea
A conventional general minor produces a conclusion like the major if the major is perpetual, but like the minor if the major is conditioned or conventional; otherwise no conclusion.

The author now examines the case where the minor is conventional general (عرفية عامة). The outcome depends on the major's modality: if the major is perpetual, the conclusion resembles the major; if the major is conditioned or conventional, the conclusion resembles the minor; with other affirmatives, no conclusion follows. This showcases the sensitivity of modal syllogisms to the specific modal types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor as conventional general
A conventional general proposition is true by custom or with implicit exceptions, often expressed with a temporal or conventional condition. For example, 'Every scholar is respected conventionally.'
This modality is weaker than perpetual but has a defined scope.
2
Distinguish based on major type
If the major is perpetual (دائمتين), the conclusion is like the major (perpetual). If the major is conditioned or conventional (مشروطة or عرفية), the conclusion is like the minor (conventional general). With other affirmative premises, no conclusion follows.
The author explicitly states: 'فالنتيجة كالكبرى لما مر على خلاف ما إذا كانت الكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين؛ إذ النتيجة كالصغرى حينئذ'.
3
Analyze the non-productive cases
When the major is not perpetual, conditioned, or conventional (e.g., possible or special), no conclusion follows. This is consistent with the earlier non-productive cases.
The 'غير من الموجبات' refers to other affirmatives not in the convertible set.
4
Apply the rule to an example
Construct a syllogism: Minor (conventional general): 'Every A is B conventionally.' Major (perpetual): 'No B is C perpetually.' Conclusion: 'No A is C perpetually.' But if major is conditioned: 'No B is C as long as B', then conclusion: 'No A is C conventionally.'
Practice by swapping the major type to see how the conclusion changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة، والكبرى من الممكنتين فذلك لا ينتج لما أن الأكبر وإن كان مسلوبًا عن الأوسط دائما فلا يدرى بأنه مسلوب عن الأصغر أم لا؛ والأوسط لا يكون متضمنا للأصغر وإن كان يمكن أنه في ضمنه. وإن كانت الصغرى مشروطة عامة والكبرى من الموجبات المنعكسة سوالبها نحو الدائمتين والعامتين والمشروطتين والعرفيتين فالنتيجة كالكبرى لما مر. فيقال مثلا بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» ما دام ج، وكل «أ» «ج» دائمًا فلا شيء من ب أ دائما. وإن كانت الكبرى من الخاصتين فالنتيجة عامة الكبرى كما يقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، وبالضرورة كل «أ» «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ» فبالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ما دام (ب). وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى عرفية خاصة. وإن كانت الصغرى عرفية عامة والكبرى من الدائمتين فالنتيجة كالكبرى لما مر على خلاف ما إذا كانت الكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين؛ إذ النتيجة كالصغرى حينئذ؛ ولا ينتج مع الغير من الموجبات. وإن كانت الصغرى من الخاصتين، والكبرى من الموجبات المنعكسة سوالبها سوى الدائمتين فالنتيجة كعكس الكبرى. فيقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل ما دام (ج)، وكل «أ» (ج) ما دام (أ) فلا شيء من «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى عرفية خاصة ولا ينتج مع الغير من الموجبات كذلك كما لا ينتج مع الدائمتين.
As for when the minor is perpetual and the major is from among the possible [propositions], then it does not produce [a conclusion] because although the major term is denied of the middle term perpetually, it is not known whether it is denied of the minor term or not; and the middle term does not encompass the minor term even though it is possible that it is encompassed by it. And if the minor is generally conditioned and the major is from among the affirmative [premises] whose negatives are convertible, like the perpetual, the general, the conditioned, and the conventional, then the conclusion is like the major for the reason mentioned. So for example it is said: necessarily, no J is B as long as J; and every A is J perpetually; therefore perpetually, no B is A perpetually. And if the major is from the two special [propositions], then the conclusion is universal like the major, as it is said: necessarily, no J is B as long as J; and necessarily, every A is J not perpetually but as long as A; therefore necessarily, no B is A as long as B. And similarly if the major is conventional special. And if the minor is conventional general and the major is from the two perpetual [propositions], then the conclusion is like the major for the reason mentioned, contrary to when the major is from the two conditioned and the two conventional; for then the conclusion is like the minor; and it does not produce [a conclusion] with other affirmative [premises]. And if the minor is from the two special [propositions] and the major is from the affirmative [premises] whose negatives are convertible except the two perpetual, then the conclusion is like the converse of the major. So it is said: necessarily, no J is B not perpetually but as long as J; and every A is J as long as A; therefore no B is A with general absoluteness for the reason mentioned. And similarly if the major is conventional special, and it does not produce [a conclusion] with other affirmative [premises] just as it does not produce with the two perpetual.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of various combinations of modal premises in a syllogism and their outcomes. The author first considers the case where the minor is perpetual (دائمة) and the major is from the possible propositions (ممكنتين). He states that no conclusion follows because although the major term is perpetually denied of the middle term, we cannot be sure it is denied of the minor term due to the lack of containment. Next, he discusses cases where the minor is generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة) and the major belongs to a set of affirmative premises whose negatives are convertible (i.e., the major is convertible when negated). In such cases, the conclusion has the same modality as the major. An example is given: a necessary universal negative major with a temporal condition ('as long as J') and a perpetual universal affirmative minor yield a perpetual universal negative conclusion. Then, if the major is from the two special propositions (خاصتين), the conclusion is universal like the major, illustrated with a temporal negative minor and a temporally limited affirmative minor producing a temporally limited negative conclusion. The author notes the same for conventional special major. Then he examines the case where the minor is conventional general (عرفية عامة) and the major is perpetual: the conclusion is like the major, but if the major is conditioned or conventional, the conclusion is like the minor. No conclusion is produced with other affirmatives. Finally, if the minor is from the two special and the major is from convertible affirmatives except the two perpetual, the conclusion is like the converse of the major. An example is given: a temporally limited negative and a temporally limited affirmative yield an absolute negative conclusion. The author emphasizes that many combinations do not yield a conclusion, highlighting the complexity of modal logic.

Key Terms
عرفية عامة
دائمتين
مشروطتين
عرفيتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the minor is conventional general and the major is conditioned, what is the modality of the conclusion?
A) Perpetual like the major.
B) Conditioned like the major.
C) Conventional general like the minor.
D) No conclusion.

Key Takeaway
With a conventional general minor, the conclusion follows the major if the major is perpetual, but follows the minor if the major is conditioned or conventional; otherwise no conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a syllogism with a conventional general minor and a conditioned major (e.g., 'No intoxicant is permitted as long as it is intoxicating'). Determine the conclusion and its modality.
?
Explain why, according to the author, no conclusion follows when the major is from the possible propositions with this minor.

When the Conclusion Is the Converse of the Major: Minor Special with Convertible Majors
Author states rule and gives example for conclusion being converse of major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive that when the minor is from the two special propositions and the major is a convertible affirmative except perpetual, the conclusion is the converse of the major.

Core Idea
A special minor with a convertible major (excluding perpetuals) yields a conclusion that is the converse of the major, not the major itself.

The final set of productive combinations involves the minor being from the two special propositions (خاصتين) and the major from the convertible affirmatives, but excluding the perpetual ones. In this case, the conclusion is not the major itself, but its converse (عكس الكبرى). The author provides an example and notes that no conclusion follows with perpetual majors or other affirmatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor as special
The two special propositions (خاصتين) are a subclass of conditioned propositions with a specific temporal limit, such as 'as long as the subject exists'. For example, 'Every A is J as long as A'.
This modality is limited in duration.
2
Select the major from convertible affirmatives except perpetuals
The major must be from the set of affirmative propositions whose negatives are convertible (e.g., general, conventional, conditioned, but not perpetual). The author excludes perpetuals (الدائمتين) because they require a different treatment.
Perpetual majors are excluded because with a special minor, the perpetual major would not yield a valid conclusion, as the author implies.
3
Observe the conclusion: converse of the major
Instead of the conclusion being the major itself, it is its converse. In the example, the major is 'Necessarily, no J is B not perpetually but as long as J', and the conclusion is 'No B is A with general absoluteness' – the converse of the major (which would be 'No B is A as long as J' but converted to absolute).
The reason given is 'لما مر' (for the reason mentioned), referencing earlier conversion rules.
4
Check the non-productive cases
The author stresses that with other majors (including perpetuals and other affirmatives not in the convertible set), no conclusion follows. This highlights the narrow scope of this rule.
This prevents overgeneralization.

Convertible Negatives and Time-Limited Premises
Author argues that negatives must be convertible and time-limited examples fail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why time-limited negative premises (non-convertible) cannot produce a valid conclusion in a categorical syllogism and identify the requirement for convertibility.

Core Idea
A negative premise must be convertible; time-limited negatives break the inference.

The author establishes a critical rule: negatives in the current syllogistic figure must be convertible. He then demonstrates that time-limited negatives (e.g., 'no human is a writer while sleeping') combined with necessary premises yield no conclusion, regardless of their position. This lesson dissects that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general requirement
The author states that negatives used in this figure must be convertible. Find this statement in the Arabic: 'أن تكون السوالب المستعملة في هذا الشكل منعكسة'. Understand why: the conclusion's validity depends on conversion.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth.
2
Analyze the first example (time-limited negative as minor)
Examine the minor: 'Necessarily, no human is a writer at the time of sleeping' (time-limited, non-convertible) and the major: 'Every rational animal is human necessarily'. The attempted conclusion is 'No writer is rational animal'. Check why this conclusion does not follow despite the premises seeming plausible.
The minor is not convertible, so the logical connection is broken.
3
Analyze the second example (time-limited negative as major)
Now the time-limited negative is the major: 'Necessarily, no jurist is a writer at the time of sleeping' with the minor 'Every writer is human necessarily'. The attempted conclusion is 'No human is a jurist'. Follow the author's reasoning that this also fails.
The same issue of non-convertibility applies regardless of premise order.
4
Understand the refutation of a potential objection
The author anticipates an objection that a particular affirmative conclusion might be derived. He clarifies that the composition assumes a negative minor, not affirmative. So no positive conclusion can be salvaged.
This clarifies that the failure is absolute—no conclusion at all, not just a weaker one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فإن من اللوازم أن تكون السوالب المستعملة في هذا الشكل منعكسة لما أن اللازم منه يفتقر في الظهور إلى الانعكاس. ولأن التركيب من الأخص وهي الوقتية من السوالب اللا منعكسة مع الضرورة لا ينتج سواء كانت الوقتية السالبة هي الصغرى في هذه الصورة أو الكبرى؛ وذلك لأن خاصة النوع إذا سلبت عنه بالضرورة لا دائمًا وحمل ذلك النوع على فصله بالضرورة فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لأنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة لا واحد من الإنسان بكاتب لا دائما بل وقت كونه نائما، وكل ناطق إنسان بالضرورة فلا يمكن أن يقال لا واحد من الكاتب بناطق أصلا. ولا يقال هب أنه لا ينتج سالبة لكن من اللوازم أن ينتج موجبة جزئية لما أن الصغرى متضمنة للموجبة الجزئية فإن الكلام في التركيب من الصغرى السالبة، والضرورية لا في الصغرى الموجبة معها، هذا إذا كانت الوقتية السالبة هي الصغرى. فأما إذا كانت هي الكبرى فكذلك. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل كاتب إنسان، وبالضرورة لا واحد من الفقيه بكاتب لا دائما بل وقت كونه نائمًا فلا يمكن أن يقال لا واحد من الإنسان بفقيه أصلا، فاعتبر بما عرفت في سائر الصور. [ت] والثالث من الأقسام فيما هو المنتج للسالبة الجزئية كما إذا كانت الصغرى من الموجبات، والكبرى من السوالب المنعكسة على عكس ذلك فإن النتيجة فيها مع الكبرى السالبة الضرورية ضرورية، ومع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة دائمة إلا في الصغرى الممكنتين مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة؛ إذ النتيجة
In summary, it is among the necessary [requirements] that the negatives used in this figure be convertible, because the necessary conclusion depends on conversion for its obviousness. And because the composition from the more specific, namely the time-limited [negatives] which are non-convertible, with necessity does not produce [a conclusion], whether the time-limited negative is the minor in this form or the major. That is because if a species' specific property is denied of it necessarily but not perpetually, and that species is predicated of its differentia necessarily, then it does not produce at all. For if we say: necessarily, no human is a writer not perpetually but at the time of his sleeping; and every rational [animal] is a human necessarily; then it cannot be said that no writer is rational at all. Nor is it said: granted that it does not produce a negative, but necessarily it produces a particular affirmative because the minor implicitly contains the particular affirmative? For the discussion is about the composition from the negative minor and the necessary [premises], not from the affirmative minor with them. This is when the time-limited negative is the minor. As for when it is the major, similarly. That is because if we say: necessarily, every writer is human; and necessarily, no jurist is a writer not perpetually but at the time of his sleeping; then it cannot be said that no human is a jurist at all. So consider what you have learned in the other forms. [T] And the third of the divisions concerns what produces the particular negative, such as when the minor is from the affirmatives and the major is from the convertible negatives, contrary to that. Then the conclusion with the necessary negative major is necessary, and with the perpetual negative major is perpetual, except in the case when the minor is from the two possible [propositions] with the perpetual negative major; for then the conclusion...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins with a general requirement: in the figure under discussion, negative premises must be convertible (i.e., their subject and predicate can be swapped). The reason is that the conclusion's validity becomes apparent only after conversion. The author then argues that combining a time-limited negative (a non-convertible proposition like 'no human is a writer at the time of his sleeping') with a necessary premise yields no conclusion, regardless of whether the time-limited negative is the minor or the major. He gives two examples: first, with the time-limited negative as minor: 'Necessarily, no human is a writer while sleeping; necessarily, every rational animal is human; therefore, no writer is rational animal' does not follow. Second, with the time-limited negative as major: 'Necessarily, every writer is human; necessarily, no jurist is a writer while sleeping; therefore, no human is a jurist' also does not follow. He then refutes the potential objection that a particular affirmative conclusion might be derived, explaining that the reasoning assumes the minor is negative, not affirmative. Finally, he begins a new section (marked [ت]) on syllogisms that produce a particular negative (سالبة جزئية). He states that when the minor is affirmative and the major is from the convertible negatives, the conclusion is like the major in modality (necessary with necessary major, perpetual with perpetual major), except when the minor is from the two possible propositions with a perpetual negative major — the text cuts off before completing the exception. This lesson is technical and presupposes familiarity with modal classifications.

Key Terms
سوالب
منعكسة
وقتية
خاصة النوع
فصل
سالبة جزئية
ممكنتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a time-limited negative premise (e.g., 'no human is a writer while sleeping') combined with a necessary premise fail to produce a conclusion?
A) Because the premises are contradictory.
B) Because the time-limited negative is not convertible.
C) Because the necessary premise is false.
D) Because the conclusion would be trivial.

Key Takeaway
Time-limited negatives are not convertible, so they cannot serve as premises that yield a necessary conclusion in this syllogistic figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Given the premises: 'Necessarily, no human is a writer while sleeping' and 'Every rational animal is human necessarily', explain step-by-step why 'No writer is rational animal' does not follow. Reference the concept of convertibility.
?
2. Create your own Islamic legal example: 'No fasting person eats intentionally at the time of fasting' (time-limited negative) and 'Every person is a fasting person necessarily' (affirmative). Test whether a conclusion follows and explain why or why not.

Requirement of Convertible Negative Premises in the Third Figure
Author argues that negative premises in the third figure must be convertible, using temporal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a universal negative premise is convertible and determine if a third figure syllogism with that premise is valid.

Core Idea
In the third figure, negative premises must be convertible; temporal negatives are not convertible and thus invalidate the syllogism.

In this passage, the author examines a crucial condition for the third figure: all negative premises must be convertible. He uses temporal negatives, which are not convertible, to demonstrate the failure to produce a conclusion. This lesson will guide you through understanding convertibility and its application in third figure syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and figure
The passage discusses the third figure. Determine which premises are negative and which are affirmative. The negative premise must be convertible for the syllogism to be valid.
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises.
2
Check convertibility of the negative premise
A universal negative (E) is convertible to a universal negative (E) only if it is not temporally qualified. Temporal negatives like 'not always but at time of sleeping' are not convertible.
Conversion: 'No S is P' becomes 'No P is S' only if the original is non-temporal.
3
Understand why temporal negatives are not convertible
Conversion requires that the truth of the original proposition implies the truth of the converse. Temporal qualification restricts the scope; e.g., 'No human is a writer at the time of sleeping' does not imply 'No writer is a human at the time of sleeping' because the latter would need to hold at all times.
The temporal condition is not symmetric.
4
Test with the author's example
Apply the rule: With a non-convertible negative minor and a necessary affirmative major, no conclusion follows. The author shows that neither a universal negative nor a particular affirmative can be validly inferred.
Example: 'No human is a writer (at sleep)' + 'Every rational is human' yields no conclusion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two eternal premises? / so the conclusion is like the converse of the major. So it is said necessarily: none of C is B, not always but as long as C, and every A is C as long as A, so none of B is A in the absolute sense, for what has passed. And upon this, if the major is a special conventional (premise), it does not produce with the other affirmative (premises) as it also does not produce with the two eternal (premises). In summary, it is necessary that the negative premises used in this figure are convertible, because what is required from it is in need of conversion for its appearance. And because the composition from the more specific, namely the temporal (premise) from the non-convertible negatives with the necessary does not produce, whether the temporal negative is the minor in this case or the major; because if the specific property of the species is denied of it necessarily but not always, and that species is predicated of its differentia necessarily, then it does not produce anything at all. For if we say: necessarily, no human is a writer not always but at the time he is sleeping, and every rational is a human necessarily, then it cannot be said: no writer is rational at all. And it cannot be said that even if it does not produce a negative, it necessarily produces a particular affirmative because the minor implies the particular affirmative; for the discussion is about composition from a negative minor and a necessary (major), not about the affirmative minor with it. This is if the temporal negative is the minor. As for if it is the major, it is similar. Because if we say: necessarily, every writer is human, and necessarily, no faqih is a writer not always but at the time he is sleeping, then it cannot be said: no human is a faqih at all. So consider with what you have known in the rest of the cases.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses a requirement in the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that in the third figure, the negative premises must be convertible (منعكسة). He begins by considering a case with two 'eternal' premises (الدائمتين) and shows that the conclusion is like the converse of the major. He then notes that if the major is a special conventional premise (عرفية خاصة), it does not yield a conclusion with other affirmatives or with two eternal premises. The key point: all negatives used in this figure must be convertible because the required conclusion depends on conversion for its appearance. He illustrates this with temporal negative premises (وقتية) that are not convertible. For example, take the premise 'Necessarily, no human is a writer not always but at the time he is sleeping'—this is a temporal negative. Combine it with 'Every rational is a human necessarily' (affirmative). The minor is temporal negative, the major is affirmative. No conclusion follows: you cannot infer 'No writer is rational' at all. One might object that even if the universal negative fails, a particular affirmative might follow because the minor implies a particular affirmative (some human is not a writer). But the author clarifies that the discussion is about composition from a negative minor and a necessary major—the affirmative minor is not present. The same applies if the temporal negative is the major, e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer is human' and 'Necessarily, no faqih is a writer not always but at the time he is sleeping'—no conclusion 'No human is a faqih' follows. The passage ends by telling the reader to apply this reasoning to other cases.

Key Terms
وقتية (waqtiyya) – A temporal premise that qualifies a negation with a specific time; not convertible.
منعكسة (mun'akisa) – Convertible premises, especially negative universals that can be converted without change in truth value.
ضرورية (daruriyya) – A necessary premise indicating a modality of necessity.

Third Figure Negative Particular Modal Syllogisms
Author lists modal combinations for negative particular conclusions in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal conclusion for a third-figure syllogism with an affirmative minor and convertible negative major, and prove it using conversion and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Modal conclusions in the third figure depend on the modality of the major premise.

The third figure can produce negative particular conclusions under specific modal conditions. This lesson examines the division where the minor is affirmative and the major is a convertible negative. You will learn which modal conclusions follow and how to prove them using two classical methods: conversion (عكس) and reductio ad absurdum (خلف). These techniques are essential for verifying modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise modalities
Examine the minor (affirmative) and major (convertible negative) premises. Note the modality: necessary, eternal, possible, conditional, etc.
The major must be a convertible negative; the minor is affirmative.
2
Determine the conclusion's modality
Apply the rule: if the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if eternal, the conclusion is eternal (except when both minors are possible, then conclusion is absolute possible).
This rule is specific to this division.
3
Prove by conversion
Convert the minor to a particular affirmative (or universal by limitation) and the major to a universal negative. The resulting first-figure syllogism yields the required conclusion.
The conversion method works for all modal combinations here.
4
Prove by reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine with the minor premise, derive a contradiction with the converted major (or with a given premise), thereby establishing the conclusion.
This method reinforces the validity without relying solely on conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من الأقسام فيما هو المنتج للسالبة الجزئية كما إذا كانت الصغرى من الموجبات، والكبرى من السوالب المنعكسة على عكس ذلك فإن النتيجة فيها مع الكبرى السالبة الضرورية ضرورية، ومع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة دائمة إلا في الصغرى الممكنتين مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة؛ إذ النتيجة فيهما ممكنة عامة على خلاف ما يكون بالعكس وهو السالبة الدائمة مع الكبرى الممكنتين، فإن ذلك عقيم. أما إذا كانت الكبرى ضرورية فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من «أ» «ج» بالضرورة فليس كل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة. وذلك لأن الصغرى تنعكس موجبة جزئية بالإطلاق العام والكبرى تنعكس سالبة كلية بالضرورة؛ والموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الضرورية من الشكل الأول ينتج سالبة جزئية ضرورية، هذا بطريق العكس. وأما بطريق الخلف فنقول إذا لم يصدق قولنا ليس كل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا كل «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام فكل «ج» «أ» بالإمكان العام؛ وقد كان لا شيء من «ج» «أ» بالضرورة وهي عكس الكبرى الضرورية، هذا خلف وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى دائمة. وأما الصغرى الممكنة مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة فالكلام فيه ظاهر بالطريقين المذكورين. وإن كانت الكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين كانت النتيجة مطلقة عامة إلا في الصغرى الممكنتين فإن النتيجة فيهما عامة. فيقال مثلا كل «ج» «ب» دائمًا، ولا شيء من «أ» «ج» بالضرورة ما دام «أ» فليس «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر من الطريقين وكذلك في الغير من المشروطتين والوقتيتين إذا كانت الصغرى فعلية. وأما إذا كانت ممكنة فكذلك لأنه لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن يكون منتجا بالإمكان العام : فاعتبر بما عرفت في كل صورة من الصور في هذا الشكل فإن البيان في الكل يمكن بطرق شتّى لما أن الأصغر محمولا على الأوسط بجهة معينة، والأوسط على الأكبر أيضًا بجهة معينة فإنه يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر والأكبر محمولا وموضوعًا في الأوسط: إما بالإيجاب، وإما بالسلب. وذلك من جملة ما يمهد طرق البيان فيتبين إذن
And the third of the divisions of what produces the negative particular (conclusion) is when the minor is from the affirmatives, and the major is from the convertible negatives, contrary to that. Thus the conclusion with the necessary negative major is necessary, and with the eternal negative major is eternal, except in the case of two possible minors with an eternal negative major; for the conclusion in them is absolute possible, contrary to what is by conversion, which is the eternal negative with two possible majors, for that is barren. As for when the major is necessary, it is said: every C is B absolutely, and no A is C necessarily, so not every B is A necessarily. That is because the minor converts to a particular affirmative in absolute sense, and the major converts to a universal negative necessary; and the particular affirmative with the necessary negative major from the first figure produces a necessary negative particular; this is by the method of conversion. As for by the method of reductio (khalf), we say: if our statement 'not every B is A necessarily' is not true, its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'every B is A in absolute possibility'. So we combine it with the minor and say: every C is B absolutely, and every B is A in absolute possibility, so every C is A in absolute possibility; but it was established that no A is C necessarily, which is the converse of the necessary major; this is a contradiction. And upon this, if the major is eternal. As for the possible minor with the eternal negative major, the discussion there is clear by the two mentioned methods. And if the major is from the conditional (mashrutata) and conventional (urfiyya), the conclusion is absolute general, except in the case of two possible minors, for the conclusion in them is general. So for example it is said: every C is B always, and no A is C necessarily as long as A, so B is not A in the absolute sense, for what has passed of the two methods. And similarly in the other among the conditional and temporal (premises) if the minor is actual. And if it is possible, it is similar, because it does not produce unless it produces in absolute possibility. So consider with what you have known in every form of the forms in this figure, for the explanation in all can be done by various methods, because the smaller term is predicated of the middle with a determined modality, and the middle upon the larger also with a determined modality, and it follows from that that the smaller and the larger are predicated and subject in the middle, either by affirmation or by negation. And this is among what paves the ways of explanation, and thus it is evident.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage identifies the third division of moods in the third figure that yield a negative particular conclusion. The scenario: the minor premise is affirmative and the major is a convertible negative (the opposite of earlier cases). The author specifies the resulting conclusion's modality: if the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if eternal, the conclusion is eternal. An exception: when both premises are possible (ممكنتين) and the major is eternal negative, the conclusion is absolute possible. The author then proves the case where the major is necessary using two methods. Method of conversion: The minor 'every C is B absolutely' converts to a particular affirmative 'some B is C absolutely'. The major 'no A is C necessarily' converts to 'no C is A necessarily' (universal negative necessary). In the first figure, a particular affirmative (some B is C) with a universal negative (no C is A) yields a particular negative 'some B is not A necessarily'. Method of reductio ad absurdum (خلف): Assume the opposite of the desired conclusion, 'every B is A in absolute possibility'. Combine this with the minor 'every C is B absolutely' to get 'every C is A in absolute possibility' (first figure). But the given major converted is 'no C is A necessarily', which contradicts the derived 'every C is A in absolute possibility' because necessity and possibility are opposed. Thus the assumption is false and the original conclusion holds. The same applies if the major is eternal. For a possible minor with eternal negative major, the same two methods work. When the major is conditional or conventional, the conclusion is absolute general, except for two possible minors which yield general possible. The author gives an example: 'every C is B always, and no A is C necessarily as long as A, therefore B is not A in the absolute sense'. He then generalizes: in all forms, since each term is connected with a specific modality, the relation between the smaller and larger term is determined via the middle term, allowing multiple methods of proof.

Reduction of Modal Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author explains reduction methods for modal categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion and reductio ad absurdum to reduce a modal categorical syllogism to the first figure.

Core Idea
Conversion and reductio unlock valid proofs in modal logic by forcing syllogisms into the first figure.

Modal syllogisms mix necessity, possibility, and absolute truth. How can we be sure they are valid? The author shows two powerful methods: conversion (ʿaks) transforms premises into first figure forms, and reductio (khalaf) proves by contradiction. You will learn to apply these step by step, using the author's own example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal syllogism
Find the two premises and the conclusion. Note the modality (necessary, possible, absolute) and quantity (universal, particular) of each categorical proposition. In the passage, the example is: 'Every J is B absolutely' and 'No A is J necessarily', concluding 'Not every B is A necessarily'.
2
Apply conversion to each premise
For each premise, apply the correct conversion rule based on its modality. The minor 'Every J is B absolutely' converts to 'Some B is J absolutely' (particular affirmative). The major 'No A is J necessarily' converts to 'No J is A necessarily' (universal negative). The converted premises now have the middle term in the correct position for the first figure.
Remember: necessary universal negative converts to necessary universal negative; absolute universal affirmative converts to absolute particular affirmative.
3
Check the new syllogism in the first figure
After conversion, the premises become: 'Some B is J absolutely' and 'No J is A necessarily'. In the first figure, the middle term (J) is subject of one premise and predicate of the other. This validly yields 'Some B is not A necessarily', which is the original conclusion. This proves the syllogism's validity.
The first figure alone yields certain conclusions without further transformation.
4
Use reductio as an alternative
If conversion seems complicated, use reductio: assume the contradictory of the conclusion ('Every B is A by general possibility'), combine it with the minor premise ('Every J is B absolutely'), derive 'Every J is A by possibility'. This contradicts the original major premise ('No J is A necessarily'), proving the original conclusion is correct.
This method works even when conversion is not straightforward, especially with different modalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيهما ممكنة عامة على خلاف ما يكون بالعكس وهو السالبة الدائمة مع الكبرى الممكنتين، فإن ذلك عقيم. أما إذا كانت الكبرى ضرورية فيقال كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من ( أ ) (ج)» بالضرورة فليس كل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة. وذلك لأن الصغرى تنعكس موجبة جزئية بالإطلاق العام والكبرى تنعكس سالبة كلية بالضرورة؛ والموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الضرورية من الشكل الأول ينتج سالبة جزئية ضرورية، هذا بطريق العكس. وأما بطريق الخلف فنقول إذا لم يصدق قولنا ليس كل «ب» «أ» بالضرورة صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا كل «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول كل «ج» «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وكل «ب» «أ» بالإمكان العام فكل «ج» [١٧٨ ] «أ» بالإمكان / العام ؛ وقد كان لا شيء من «ج» «أ» بالضرورة وهي عكس و] الكبرى الضرورية، هذا خلف وعلى هذا إذا كانت الكبرى دائمة. وأما الصغرى الممكنة مع الكبرى السالبة الدائمة فالكلام فيه ظاهر بالطريقين المذكورين. وإن كانت الكبرى من المشروطتين والعرفيتين كانت النتيجة مطلقة عامة إلا في الصغرى الممكنتين فإن النتيجة فيهما عامة. فيقال مثلا كل ((ج)) ((ب)) دائمًا، ولا شيء من «أ» (ج) بالضرورة ما دام «أ» فليس «ب» «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر من الطريقين وكذلك في الغير من المشروطتين والوقتيتين إذا كانت الصغرى فعلية. وأما إذا كانت ممكنة فكذلك لأنه لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن يكون منتجا بالإمكان العام : فاعتبر بما عرفت في كل صورة من الصور في هذا الشكل فإن البيان في الكل يمكن بطرق شتّى لما أن الأصغر محمولا على الأوسط بجهة معينة، والأوسط على الأكبر أيضًا بجهة معينة فإنه يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر والأكبر محمولا وموضوعًا في الأوسط: إما بالإيجاب، وإما بالسلب. وذلك من جملة ما يمهد طرق البيان فيتبين إذن بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول؛ ثم عكس النتيجة أو بعكس الصغرى والكبرى ليرتد إلى الأول كذلك، أو بعكس الصغرى فقط ليرتد إلى الثاني، أو بعكس الكبرى فقط ليرتد إلى الثالث. وعلى هذا فإنه يمكن بالغير أيضًا على ما عرف
In both are possible general contrary to what is by conversion, which is the permanent negative with the two possible majors, for that is sterile. As for when the major is necessary, it is said: Every J is B absolutely, and no A is J necessarily, so not every B is A necessarily. This is because the minor converts to an affirmative particular absolute, and the major converts to a universal negative necessary; and the particular affirmative with the necessary universal negative from the first figure produces a particular negative necessary. This is by the method of conversion. As for the method of reductio, we say: If our statement 'not every B is A necessarily' is not true, its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'every B is A by general possibility'. Then we join it to the minor and say: Every J is B absolutely, and every B is A by general possibility, so every J is A by general possibility, but it was that no J is A necessarily, which is the converse of the necessary major. This is a contradiction. And likewise if the major is permanent. As for the possible minor with the permanent negative major, the discussion is clear by the two mentioned methods. And if the major is of the conditional or conventional types, the conclusion is absolute general except in the two possible minors, for the conclusion in them is general. For example, it is said: Every J is B always, and no A is J necessarily as long as A is, so not every B is A absolutely, according to the two methods mentioned. Similarly for other conditional and time-restricted propositions if the minor is actual. As for if it is possible, likewise, because it is productive only if it is productive by general possibility. So consider what you have known in every case of the cases in this figure, for the demonstration in all can be by various methods, since the minor is predicated of the middle with a certain modality, and the middle is also predicated of the major with a certain modality, then it follows that the minor and major are predicated and subject in the middle: either by affirmation or negation. And this is part of what paves the ways for demonstration. So it becomes clear, then, by making the minor a major and the major a minor to reduce to the first figure; then converting the conclusion, or by converting the minor and the major to reduce to the first similarly, or by converting only the minor to reduce to the second, or by converting only the major to reduce to the third. And likewise it is possible by other means as known.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of how to handle modal categorical syllogisms, specifically when premises have different modalities (necessary, possible, absolute, permanent, conditional). The author demonstrates reduction methods to the first figure using conversion (عكس) and reductio ad absurdum (خلف). The discussion proceeds case by case. First, a sterile case is noted: a possible major and a permanent negative minor yield nothing. Then, when the major is necessary, the minor is absolute universal affirmative, and the conclusion is drawn by conversion: the minor becomes particular affirmative absolute, the major becomes universal negative necessary, and in first figure they produce particular negative necessary. Then the reductio method is shown: if the conclusion is denied, its contradictory (every B is A by possibility) is assumed, and combined with the minor, yielding a contradiction with the original necessary major. The same method works for permanent major. For possible minor with permanent negative major, the result is obvious by the two methods. When the major is conditional or conventional, the conclusion is absolute general except when the minor is possible; then the conclusion is general. An example with 'always' and 'as long as' is given. The author then generalizes: in any modal combination in this figure, the minor is predicated of the middle with a certain modality, and the middle of the major, so the relationship can be demonstrated by various methods, including swapping premises, conversion, etc. The passage ends by listing four reduction methods: swapping premises (making minor major and vice versa) then converting conclusion; converting both premises; converting only the minor to reduce to second figure; converting only the major to reduce to third figure. This is a technical exposition expecting familiarity with the figures and conversion rules.

بالضرورة
