The Nature of Conditional Syllogisms
Introduction to conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conditional syllogisms from categorical syllogisms and identify the two types of conditional propositions and their relations.

Core Idea
Conditional conclusions require conditional premises; categoricals can be reached either way.

In this lesson, we begin the third type of syllogism: conditional syllogisms. The author explains that just as premises can be categorical or conditional, so too conclusions. Most importantly, a conditional conclusion can only be obtained through a conditional syllogism, while categorical conclusions may come from either type. We will also explore the two fundamental relations in conditionals: following (mutābaʿa) and opposition (muʿānada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim about conditional syllogisms
Find the sentence where the author states that conditional syllogisms are necessary for conditional conclusions. Contrast this with categorical syllogisms, which can produce both categorical and conditional conclusions.
The key phrase: 'conditionals cannot be obtained except by conditional syllogisms.'
2
Distinguish pure and mixed conditional syllogisms
Look for the author's classification: conditional syllogisms may be composed entirely of conditional premises (pure) or mixed with categorical premises. This affects their form.
3
Recognize the two relations in conditional propositions
Identify the two relations: 'following' (mutābaʿa) in conjunctive conditionals, and 'opposition' (muʿānada) in disjunctive conditionals. These were previously covered in the discussion of muttasilāt and munfaṣilāt.
Also note the distinction between complete connection (implication from both sides) and incomplete (one side).
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Given a conditional syllogism from kalam, such as: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator; if it has a creator, then it is contingent; therefore, if the world is originated, then it is contingent.' Identify that this is a pure conditional syllogism (all conditionals) and the relation is 'following'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣٠٤] النوع الثالث : في القياسات الشرطية . ولما كانت المقدّمات منها حملية، ومنها شرطية فكذلك المطالب منها حملية، ومنها شرطية، وكما أن من الحمليّات منها ما لا يفتقر في التحقق إلى قياس، ومنها ما يفتقر . فكذلك الشرطيات فإن منها ما لا يفتقر، ومنها ما يفتقر في الطبيعيات والرياضيات وغيرها متصلة ومنفصلة والحمليات مما يمكن تحصيلها بقياسات حملية وقياسات شرطية على خلاف الشرطيات فإنه لا يمكن تحصيلها إلا بقياسات شرطية. فههنا إذن قياسات شرطية نتائجها شرطية؛ وتلك القياسات قد تكون من شرطيات صرفة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون من مقدّمات مختلطة. والقضايا الشرطية توافق القضايا الحملية في أنها أقوال جازمة قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب، وتخالفها في أنها مركبة بالضرورة من أجزاء فيها تأليف جزئي. ومع ذلك فإن النسبة بينها ليست بنسبة أن يقال في الإيجاب إن أولها ثانيها كما يقال إن الإنسان عالم فيوافقها إذن في أن هناك حكما بنسبة جزء إلى جزء، ويخالفه في هيئة ذلك الحكم لكنها أي الشرطيات مختلفة أيضًا في هذه النسبة. فتكون النسبة الإيجابية في البعض منها على سبيل / «المتابعة»، وفي البعض منها على سبيل «المعاندة» كما مرّ من قبل في بيان [۱۷۸ظ] المتصلات والمنفصلات. وقد مرَّ أيضًا أن الاتصال إما تام وذلك فيما يكون اللزوم من الجانبين، وإما غير تام وذلك فيما يكون من جانب واحد. وكذلك
[304] The third type: On conditional syllogisms. Since premises are either categorical or conditional, likewise the sought conclusions are either categorical or conditional. And just as among categorical propositions there are those that do not need a syllogism for verification and those that do, similarly among conditionals there are those that do not need and those that need — in physics, mathematics, and others — conjunctive and disjunctive. And categoricals can be obtained by categorical syllogisms and conditional syllogisms, unlike conditionals, which cannot be obtained except by conditional syllogisms. Here, then, are conditional syllogisms whose conclusions are conditional. Those syllogisms may be from pure conditionals, or they may not be but rather from mixed premises. Conditional propositions agree with categorical propositions in that they are decisive statements capable of being affirmed or denied, and differ from them in that they are necessarily composed of parts that have a partial composition. Moreover, the relation between them is not such that it is said in affirmation that the first is the second, as it is said 'man is knowledgeable'. So they agree in that there is a judgment relating part to part, but differ in the form of that judgment. However, conditionals themselves differ in this relation. For the affirmative relation in some of them is in the manner of 'following', and in some of them in the manner of 'opposition', as has been previously mentioned in the discussion of conjunctives and disjunctives. And it has also been mentioned that connection (ittiṣāl) is either complete, which is when implication is from both sides, or incomplete, which is when it is from one side. Likewise...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new section on conditional syllogisms (القياسات الشرطية). The author first establishes the context: just as premises can be categorical or conditional, so too conclusions can be categorical or conditional. Some propositions are self-evident and need no syllogism, others do. Importantly, while categorical conclusions can be obtained by either categorical or conditional syllogisms, conditional conclusions require conditional syllogisms. The author then defines conditional syllogisms as those whose conclusions are conditional, and notes they may be pure (all premises conditional) or mixed (with categoricals). Next, a comparison is made between conditional and categorical propositions: both are decisive and truth-apt, but conditionals are necessarily composite with a partial composition. The relation in a conditional is not a simple subject-predicate attribution (like 'man is knowledgeable') but a relation between two parts. Moreover, conditionals themselves differ: some have a relation of 'following' (متابعة) and others of 'opposition' (معاندة), referring to conjunctive (متصلات) and disjunctive (منفصلات) conditionals introduced earlier. Finally, the author recalls that connection (اتصال) can be complete (implication from both sides) or incomplete (from one side only). This sets the stage for detailed rules of conditional syllogisms.

Conditional syllogisms are the only means to derive conditional conclusions, and they rely on two relations: following (in conjunctives) and opposition (in disjunctives).

Introduction to Conditional Syllogisms: Classification and Necessity
Introduction and classification of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify syllogisms as categorical or conditional, and conditional syllogisms as pure or mixed, based on the types of premises.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are the only way to prove conditional conclusions.

In this passage, the author expands the scope of syllogism to include conditional ones. Just as categorical syllogisms prove categorical statements, conditional syllogisms are required to prove conditional statements. This lesson introduces the fundamental classification and explains why conditional syllogisms are indispensable in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition types
Recognize that premises can be either categorical (حملیة) or conditional (شرطیة). The same applies to conclusions.
2
Correlate conclusion with syllogism type
Note that categorical conclusions may be obtained by either categorical or conditional syllogisms, but conditional conclusions can only be obtained by conditional syllogisms.
This is the key point establishing the necessity of studying conditional syllogisms.
3
Distinguish pure vs. mixed conditional syllogisms
Understand that conditional syllogisms may consist entirely of conditional premises (صرفة) or combine categorical and conditional premises (مختلطة).
The author does not detail further in this passage, but the distinction is foundational.
4
Apply the classification
Given an example like 'If the world is originated, it has an originator', determine what type of syllogism is needed to prove it.
The conclusion is conditional, so a conditional syllogism is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : في القياسات الشرطية ولما كانت المقدّمات منها حملية، ومنها شرطية فكذلك المطالب منها حملية، ومنها شرطية، وكما أن من الحمليّات منها ما لا يفتقر في التحقق إلى قياس، ومنها ما يفتقر . فكذلك الشرطيات فإن منها ما لا يفتقر، ومنها ما يفتقر في الطبيعيات والرياضيات وغيرها متصلة ومنفصلة والحمليات مما يمكن تحصيلها بقياسات حملية وقياسات شرطية على خلاف الشرطيات فإنه لا يمكن تحصيلها إلا بقياسات شرطية. فههنا إذن قياسات شرطية نتائجها شرطية؛ وتلك القياسات قد تكون من شرطيات صرفة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون من مقدّمات مختلطة.
The third type: on conditional syllogisms. Since premises are either categorical or conditional, so also the sought conclusions are either categorical or conditional. And just as among categoricals there are those that do not need a syllogism to be realized and those that do, so also among conditionals there are those that do not need and those that need in natural sciences and mathematics and others, conjunctive and disjunctive. And categoricals can be obtained by categorical syllogisms and conditional syllogisms, unlike conditionals which cannot be obtained except by conditional syllogisms. So here there are conditional syllogisms whose conclusions are conditional; and those syllogisms may be from pure conditionals, or may not be but from mixed premises.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces the third type of syllogism: conditional syllogisms (القياسات الشرطية). The author begins by drawing an analogy with categorical syllogisms: just as premises and conclusions can be either categorical or conditional, some propositions require a syllogism to be proven while others do not. Conditional propositions, like categoricals, are needed in various sciences (natural sciences, mathematics) and come in two types: conjunctive (متصلات) and disjunctive (منفصلات). A key distinction is that while categorical conclusions can be obtained through either categorical or conditional syllogisms, conditional conclusions can only be obtained through conditional syllogisms. This establishes the necessity of studying conditional syllogisms. Finally, the author notes that conditional syllogisms themselves can be 'pure' (صرفة), composed entirely of conditional premises, or 'mixed' (مختلطة), combining categorical and conditional premises. An example: to prove 'If a person is just, his testimony is accepted' (a conditional), one must use a conditional syllogism, perhaps with a mixed premise like 'Every just person's testimony is accepted' (categorical).

The Three Types of Conditional Syllogisms
Author classifies conditional syllogisms into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of conditional syllogisms: exceptive, conjunctive, and composite.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms come in three forms: exceptive, conjunctive, and a composite of both.

In this passage, the author lays out a key classification for conditional syllogisms. Not every conditional proposition is self-evident; many require proof through one of three types of conditional syllogisms. Understanding this classification helps you recognize the logical structure behind many arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the Arabic enumeration: the first type is exceptive (istithna'i), the second is conjunctive (iqtirani), and the third is composite (murakkab). Note the author's phrasing: 'among them are those that are exceptive... among them are those that are conjunctive... among them are those that are not purely exceptive nor purely conjunctive but composed of the two.'
The composite type is exemplified by proof by contradiction (khalf).
2
Understand the exceptive syllogism
An exceptive syllogism has a conditional premise and an 'exception' that affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent. For example, 'If nabidh is intoxicating, it is forbidden; it is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden.' This is modus ponens.
3
Understand the conjunctive syllogism
Here, 'conjunctive' refers to a conditional syllogism with two conditional premises and a conditional conclusion. No exception is made. For instance, 'If it is wine, it is intoxicating. If it is intoxicating, it is forbidden. Therefore, if it is wine, it is forbidden.'
This is a chain of conditionals (hypothetical syllogism).
4
Understand the composite syllogism
The composite type combines elements of both exceptive and conjunctive. The author mentions proof by contradiction (khalf) as an example: you assume the opposite, derive a contradiction, and thereby affirm the original. This involves an exceptive step within a chain.
5
Determine which is closest to nature
The passage begins to say that one type is 'closest to nature' (al-tab'). Although the text cuts off, the order of listing (exceptive first) may suggest that the exceptive is most intuitive. Reflect on why that might be: it mirrors everyday reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القسم الأول من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من متصلتين ] قد مر من قبل أن القضية الشرطية قد تكون بديهية، وقد لا تكون، وما لا يكون فذلك مفتقرًا إلى ما يحرره ويقرره في الأذهان؛ وذلك من الأقيسة الشرطية والأقيسة الشرطية على أنواع كثيرة: [1] منها ما يكون من الأقيسة الاستثنائية، [ب] ومنها ما يكون من الأقيسة الاقترانية، [ت] ومنها ما لا يكون من الاستثنائية الصرفة ولا من الاقترانية الصرفة كذلك بل يكون مركبا منهما كما مرَّ في الخلف. وما هو الأقرب من هذه الجملة إلى الطبع فذلك ما يكون
Chapter: [ On the first division of the five divisions regarding what is composed of two conditionals ] It has passed before that the conditional proposition may be self-evident, and may not be, and what is not requires what establishes and settles it in minds; and that is from conditional syllogisms. And conditional syllogisms are of many types: [1] among them are those that are exceptive syllogisms, [b] among them are those that are conjunctive syllogisms, [t] among them are those that are not purely exceptive nor purely conjunctive but rather are composed of the two as passed in proof by contradiction. And what is closest among these to nature is that which is...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a classification of conditional syllogisms (aqyisah shartiyyah). The author begins by stating that conditional propositions may be self-evident (badihiyyah) or not; those that are not require establishment through conditional syllogisms. The author then enumerates the types of conditional syllogisms. The first type is the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), which has the form 'if p then q, but p, therefore q' (or 'if p then q, but not q, therefore not p'). The second type is the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), typically a categorical syllogism, but here it refers to a conditional syllogism that is conjunctive? Actually, in the context of conditional syllogisms, 'iqtirani' often refers to a syllogism composed of two conditional premises with a common term, leading to a conditional conclusion. However, the author may be using 'iqtirani' in a broader sense. The third type is a composite syllogism that is neither purely exceptive nor purely conjunctive but a combination of both, as seen in proof by contradiction (al-khalf). The author then notes that the one closest to nature (al-tab') is... but the text cuts off. The passage sets the stage for a detailed discussion of these types of conditional syllogisms.

Conditional Syllogisms Composed of Two Conditionals: Figures
Author classifies conditional syllogisms and explains the first type composed of two conditionals with its figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional syllogisms and identify the four figures in a conditional syllogism composed of two conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms mirror categorical ones: when the middle term is a complete part, four figures emerge.

We have already seen categorical syllogisms and their figures. Now the author extends that framework to syllogisms whose premises are conditional propositions (if-then). Not all conditional syllogisms are alike; some are exceptive, some conjunctive, and among the conjunctive, those composed of two conditionals can be arranged in figures identical to the categorical ones. This lesson walks you through that classification and the key condition for productivity: the middle term must be a complete part in both premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall classification of conditional syllogisms
Find in the Arabic how the author divides conditional syllogisms into exceptive (istithnā'iyya), conjunctive (iqtirāniyya), and composite (khalf).
Look for the terms: الاستثنائية, الاقترانية, الخلف.
2
Understand the five types of conjunctive conditional syllogisms
The author lists five types based on the combination of categorical and conditional premises. Focus on the first: composed of two conditionals (muttasilāt).
The list: two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, conditional+disjunctive.
3
Focus on the first type: two conditionals
In a syllogism of two conditionals, the middle term (common part) can be arranged in three ways: complete in both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. Identify these in the text.
Complete part means the entire antecedent or consequent; incomplete means part of a compound like a conjunction.
4
Recognize the productive arrangement
The author states that only when the middle term is a complete part in both premises does the syllogism produce a conclusion. This arrangement yields four figures.
The four figures depend on whether the middle term is antecedent or consequent in the minor and major premises.
5
Apply the figures
For each figure, note the position of the middle term: Fig.1: consequent in minor, antecedent in major; Fig.2: consequent in both; Fig.3: antecedent in both; Fig.4: antecedent in minor, consequent in major.
The conditions for validity and moods are analogous to categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القسم الأول من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من متصلتين ] قد مر من قبل أن القضية الشرطية قد تكون بديهية، وقد لا تكون، وما لا يكون فذلك مفتقرًا إلى ما يحرره ويقرره في الأذهان؛ وذلك من الأقيسة الشرطية والأقيسة الشرطية على أنواع كثيرة: [1] منها ما يكون من الأقيسة الاستثنائية، [ب] ومنها ما يكون من الأقيسة الاقترانية، [ت] ومنها ما لا يكون من الاستثنائية الصرفة ولا من الاقترانية الصرفة كذلك بل يكون مركبا منهما كما مرَّ في الخلف. وما هو الأقرب من هذه الجملة إلى الطبع فذلك ما يكون من الاستثنائية أو من الاقترانية. وإنها أي الاقترانية على أنواع كثيرة كذلك: منها ما يكون مركبا من المتّصلات، ومنها ما يكون مركبًا من المنفصلات، ومنها ما لا يكون من المتصلات المحضة ولا من المنفصلات كذلك بل يكون مركبا منهما؛ وما هو الأقرب منها إلى الطبع فذلك ما يتركب من المتصلات وهو قسم واحد من الأقيسة الشرطية الاقترانية؛ إذ هي على خمسة أقسام بالنسبة إلى ما يتركب من القضايا الحملية والشرطية وهي هذه: ما يتركب من متصلتين، وما يتركب من منفصلتين، أو من حملية ومتصلة، أو من حملية ومنفصلة، أو من متّصلة ومنفصلة. والأول منها وهو ما يتركب من متصلتين فذلك على ثلاثة أقسام بالنسبة إلى ما هو المشترك بينهما وهو الحد الأوسط؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون جزءًا تاما من كل واحدة من المقدمتين وهو أحد الطرفين من كل واحدة منهما سواء كان مقدَّما أو تاليا، أو لا يكون بل يكون جزءا غير تام من كل واحدة من المقدمتين وهو أحد الطرفي كل واحدة منهما، أو تاما في إحدى المقدمتين غير تام في المقدمة الأخرى. والمنتج منها لا محالة هو الأول وهو أن يكون المشترك جزءا تاما في المقدمتين؛ وإنه من جملة ما ينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة. وذلك لأن المشترك فيه لا يخلو من أن يكون تاليا في الصغرى مقدما في الكبرى وهو الشكل الأول، أو تاليا فيهما وهو الشكل الثاني أو مقدما فيهما وهو الشكل الثالث، أو مقدما في الصغرى تاليا في الكبرى وهو الشكل الرابع. ثم شرائط الإنتاج وعدد الضروب والنتائج في كل شكل من هذه الأشكال مثل ما قد مرَّ في الأشكال التي مرّ ذكرها في الحمليَّات.
Chapter: On the first of the five divisions concerning what is composed of two conditionals. It has passed before that a conditional proposition may be self-evident or not, and what is not requires something to establish and settle it in the minds; and that is through conditional syllogisms. Conditional syllogisms are of many types: [1] some are from exceptive syllogisms (istithnā'iyya), [b] some are from conjunctive syllogisms (iqtirāniyya), [t] some are neither purely exceptive nor purely conjunctive but composite of both, as in reductio (khalf). And what is closest to nature among these is what is exceptive or conjunctive. And the conjunctive syllogisms are also of many types: some are composed of conditionals, some of disjunctives, some are neither purely conditionals nor purely disjunctives but composite of both; and what is closest to nature among them is what is composed of conditionals, which is one division of conjunctive conditional syllogisms. For they are of five divisions with respect to composition from categorical and conditional propositions: those composed of two conditionals, those composed of two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. The first of these, which is composed of two conditionals, is of three divisions with respect to what is common between them, which is the middle term: because it cannot be but either (1) a complete part of each of the two premises, being one of the two extremes (either antecedent or consequent) in each; (2) or not, but an incomplete part of each premise, being one of the two extremes of each; (3) or complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. The productive one of these inevitably is the first, where the common term is a complete part in both premises; and it is among what yields the four figures. That is because the common term cannot be but either consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major — that is the first figure; or consequent in both — that is the second figure; or antecedent in both — that is the third figure; or antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major — that is the fourth figure. Then the conditions of productivity, the number of moods, and the conclusions in each of these figures are like what has passed in the figures mentioned for categoricals.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional syllogisms (al-aqyisa al-shartiyya). It begins by noting that conditional propositions may be self-evident (badihiyya) or not; non-evident ones require proof via conditional syllogisms. These syllogisms are divided into three types: (1) exceptive (istithnā'iyya — modus ponens/tollens), (2) conjunctive (iqtirāniyya — where the conclusion is not separated), and (3) compound of both, used in reductio (khalf). The most natural among them are the exceptive and conjunctive. The conjunctive syllogisms are further divided: some are composed of conditionals (muttasilāt), some of disjunctives (munfasilāt), and some are mixed. The most natural is the purely conditional. Conditional conjunctive syllogisms themselves come in five types depending on the combination of categorical (hamliyya) and conditional premises: (a) two conditionals, (b) two disjunctives, (c) categorical + conditional, (d) categorical + disjunctive, (e) conditional + disjunctive. The focus of this chapter is the first type: composed of two conditionals. In such a syllogism, the middle term (al-hadd al-awsaṭ) — the part common to both premises — can be arranged in three ways: (1) it is a complete part (i.e., an entire antecedent or consequent) in both premises; (2) it is an incomplete part (i.e., part of a conjunction or the like) in both; (3) it is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. Only the first arrangement is productive. In that arrangement, the middle term, being a complete part in both premises, can occupy four positions relative to the minor and major premises, generating the four figures analogous to categorical syllogisms: Figure I: middle is consequent in minor and antecedent in major; Figure II: middle is consequent in both; Figure III: middle is antecedent in both; Figure IV: middle is antecedent in minor and consequent in major. The conditions for validity, number of moods, and conclusions in each figure follow the same rules as in categorical syllogisms. The author thus extends the theory of the four figures from categorical logic to a certain class of conditional logic.

Key Terms
قياس شرطي
قياس اقتراني
حد أوسط
متصل
شكل

Author enumerates the five types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms come in five distinct flavours based on premise combinations.

In this passage, the author classifies conditional conjunctive syllogisms into five types. This classification helps us understand the variety of valid argument forms in Islamic logic. By mastering these types, you will be able to recognise the structure of complex arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Three Types of Syllogisms with Two Conditional Premises
Author divides syllogisms with two conditional premises into three types based on the middle term's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish and classify conditional syllogisms based on whether the middle term is a complete part, an incomplete part, or mixed.

Core Idea
The middle term in conditional syllogisms is a propositional component — complete (entire antecedent/consequent) or incomplete (part of it).

In the previous lesson, we learned that syllogisms can be composed of two conditional premises. The author now expands on this by dividing such syllogisms into three types based on the role of the middle term. This classification is essential for determining which forms yield valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
Locate the two 'if-then' statements that serve as the premises of the syllogism. Each premise is a conditional proposition with an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali).
2
Locate the middle term
Find what is common between the two premises — this is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). In conditional syllogisms, the middle term is not a single term but a proposition or part of a proposition that appears in both premises.
The middle term could be an entire antecedent or consequent, or a part thereof.
3
Classify the middle term's status in each premise
For each premise, determine whether the middle term is a complete part (the whole antecedent or consequent) or an incomplete part (only a portion of that). Then match to one of three types: (1) complete in both premises, (2) incomplete in both premises, or (3) complete in one and incomplete in the other.
The three types correspond to the three divisions enumerated by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول منها وهو ما يتركب من متصلتين فذلك على ثلاثة أقسام بالنسبة إلى ما هو المشترك بينهما وهو الحد الأوسط؛ وذلك لأنه [١] لا يخلو من أن يكون جزءًا تاما من كل واحدة من المقدمتين وهو أحد الطرفين من كل واحدة منهما سواء كان مقدَّما أو تاليا، [۲] أو لا يكون بل يكون جزءا غير تام من كل واحدة من المقدمتين وهو أحد الطرفي كل واحدة منهما، [٣] أو تاما في إحدى المقدمتين غير تام في المقدمة الأخرى.

That is because it [1] either is a complete part of each of the two premises, i.e., one of the two extremes from each of them, whether it is antecedent or consequent; [2] or it is not, but rather is an incomplete part of each of the two premises, i.e., one of the two extremes of each of them; [3] or it is complete in one of the two premises and incomplete in the other premise.

Commentary
This passage focuses on the first of the five types mentioned earlier: syllogisms composed of two conditional premises. The author divides these into three categories based on the common term (al-mushtarak) which is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). The three possibilities are: (1) The middle term is a complete part of each premise, meaning it is one of the two extremes (either antecedent or consequent) of both conditionals. (2) The middle term is an incomplete part of each premise, meaning it is not the whole antecedent or consequent but a part of it. (3) The middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. This classification is crucial for determining which forms are productive.

The Productive Conditional Syllogism and Its Four Figures
Author identifies the productive type and shows it forms four figures similar to categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given two-conditional syllogism is productive and determine its figure based on the position of the common term.

Core Idea
Only the first type of two-conditional syllogism is productive; it mirrors the four figures of categoricals.

Not all conditional syllogisms yield conclusions. In this passage, the author specifies which type is productive and explains how the four figures arise from the position of the common term. Understanding this allows you to quickly evaluate the validity of complex conditional arguments in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term
Find the term that appears in both conditional premises. In a two-conditional syllogism, the common term must be a complete part (either the whole antecedent or the whole consequent) in each premise.
If the common term is not a complete part, the syllogism is unproductive.
2
Check if it is a complete part in both premises
Verify that the common term is either the entire antecedent or entire consequent in each premise. This ensures the syllogism is of the productive type.
3
Determine its position in minor and major
Identify whether the common term functions as antecedent (if) or consequent (then) in the minor premise (first conditional) and major premise (second conditional).
The minor premise is usually the one containing the subject of the conclusion.
4
Classify the figure
Use the following mapping: first figure = consequent in minor, antecedent in major; second figure = consequent in both; third figure = antecedent in both; fourth figure = antecedent in minor, consequent in major.
This mirrors the categorical figures exactly.
5
Recall validity conditions from categoricals
The conditions for productivity, number of moods, and conclusions in each figure are the same as those already discussed for categorical syllogisms. Apply those rules here.
For example, in the first figure only certain moods are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنتج منها لا محالة هو الأول وهو أن يكون المشترك جزءا تاما في المقدمتين؛ وإنه من جملة ما ينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة. وذلك لأن المشترك فيه لا يخلو من أن يكون تاليا في الصغرى مقدما في الكبرى وهو الشكل الأول، أو تاليا فيهما وهو الشكل الثاني أو مقدما فيهما وهو الشكل الثالث، أو مقدما في الصغرى تاليا في الكبرى وهو الشكل الرابع. ثم شرائط الإنتاج وعدد الضروب والنتائج في كل شكل من هذه الأشكال مثل ما قد مرَّ في الأشكال التي مرّ ذكرها في الحمليَّات.
And the productive among them inevitably is the first, which is that the common [term] is a complete part in both premises; and it is among that in which the four figures are formed. That is because the common [term] in it is either a consequent in the minor [premise] and an antecedent in the major, which is the first figure; or a consequent in both, which is the second figure; or an antecedent in both, which is the third figure; or an antecedent in the minor and a consequent in the major, which is the fourth figure. Then the conditions of productivity and the number of moods and the conclusions in each figure of these figures are like what has been given in the figures mentioned in the categoricals.

Commentary
This passage identifies which of the three types of two-conditional syllogisms is productive: only the first type, where the middle term is a complete part in both premises. This type can form the four figures. The author describes each figure based on the position of the common term (antecedent or consequent) in the minor and major premises. For example, if the common term is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, it is the first figure. The figures correspond to the categorical syllogism figures. The conditions of productivity, number of moods, and conclusions for each figure are the same as those already discussed for categorical syllogisms.

Four Moods of First Figure Conditional Syllogisms
Enumerates the four moods of the first conditional figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four moods of the first figure in conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
First figure conditional syllogisms are complete: their conclusions follow directly without conversion.

In this passage, the author lays out the four moods of the first figure for conditional syllogisms. These moods parallel the categorical moods but use conditional premises. Understanding them is crucial because they provide the foundation for many arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, especially those involving causal chains.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of the first conditional figure
The first figure has the middle term subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major. Here, each mood consists of two conditional statements sharing a common middle condition (CD).
2
Identify the mood from the quantifiers
Each mood is defined by the quantity (universal vs. particular) and quality (affirmative vs. negative) of the two premises. The first mood is universal affirmative + universal affirmative, second is universal affirmative + universal negative, etc.
3
Map the variables to the middle term
In the passage, 'AB' is the minor term, 'EZ' is the major, and 'CD' is the middle. The conclusion always relates AB to EZ via CD.
4
Verify that each mood yields the correct conclusion
Check each mood: first mood (both universal affirmative) gives universal affirmative; second (universal affirmative + universal negative) gives universal negative; third (particular affirmative + universal affirmative) gives particular affirmative; fourth (particular affirmative + universal negative) gives particular negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضروب الأربعة في الشكل الأول قياسات كاملة بالنسبة إلى المطالب الأربعة وإنتاجها بين بذاتها لما أن الأوسط في كل ضرب منها متضمن للأصغر ومستلزم للأكبر. فالضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان «ج د» فـ «هز» ينتج قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «هز». والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز» . والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (ج (د)، وكلما كان (ج) ده فـ هـ ز» ينتج قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (د) هـز». والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان ج) د) فـ هـز» / ينتج قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان أب» فـ «هز»؛ فهذه الضروب ستة بنفسها في الإنتاج فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لما يتبين به الصدق في النتائج على خلاف ما يكون من الضروب المنتجة في الأشكال الباقية فإن ذلك من جملة ما له حاجة إلى البيان؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
And the four moods in the first figure are complete syllogisms with respect to the four demands, and their production is evident in themselves because the middle in each mood contains the minor and implies the major. The first mood: our saying 'Whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'Whenever CD is, then EZ is' yields our saying 'Whenever AB is, then EZ is'. The second mood: our saying 'Whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever CD is, then EZ is' yields our saying 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever AB is, then EZ is'. The third mood: our saying 'It may be that whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'Whenever CD is, then EZ is' yields our saying 'It may be that whenever AB is, then EZ is'. The fourth mood: our saying 'It may be that whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever CD is, then EZ is' yields our saying 'It may not be that whenever AB is, then EZ is'. So these four moods are by themselves productive, so there is no need to address what makes the conclusions true, unlike the productive moods in the remaining figures, for that is among things that need demonstration; and that will be known later.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage provides the four moods of the first figure in conditional syllogisms composed of two conditionals. The author states that these moods are 'complete' (kamila) meaning their productivity is self-evident because the middle term contains the minor and implies the major. He then gives each mood with an example using placeholder variables: The first mood is universal affirmative in both premises, yielding a universal affirmative conclusion. The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The third mood has a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The fourth mood has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. These correspond to the four moods of the first categorical figure. The author adds that these four moods are inherently productive, unlike moods in other figures that require demonstration (e.g., conversion) to prove their validity.

قياسات كاملة

Objection and Rebuttal on Middle Term Identity
Author addresses a potential objection that the middle term must be identical, and begins a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the middle term must be identical in the first figure and evaluate the author's rebuttal by examining the logical relations between terms.

Core Idea
The middle term does not need literal identity; its logical relations suffice for syllogistic validity.

Even the most evident syllogisms can face objections. One such objection claims that the first mood of the first figure only works if the middle term is identical in both premises. In this passage, the author rejects that requirement, arguing that identity would actually lead to impossible logical relationships. This lesson unpacks that objection and the beginning of the author's critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that the first mood of the first figure only produces a conclusion if the middle term is identical (mutahid) in both premises. Locate this claim in the Arabic text.
The objection is at the beginning of the passage after 'that it may be said regarding the first mood...'
2
Understand the author's stance
The author immediately denies this necessity, calling it 'subject to denial'. Note that he does not accept the condition of identity.
3
Analyze the logical impossibility
The author argues that if the middle term were identical, it would have to be both a consequent of the minor and a necessary condition for the major, which is impossible given that the major is more general than the middle and the middle more general than the minor.
This step requires careful reading: the author explains that the major (largest term) is often more general than the middle, so the middle cannot be a necessary condition for the major.
4
Recognize the incomplete rebuttal
The passage is cut off, but the author continues with another possibility. Note that the full rebuttal likely extends to later pages to fully refute the objection.
This incompleteness is acknowledged in the source review.
5
Apply the critique to a concrete example
Consider a fiqh syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; all nabidh is intoxicating; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' The middle term 'intoxicating' is logically identical in meaning, not necessarily verbatim. Determine if the objection's condition holds here.
This step helps connect the abstract discussion to Islamic sciences.

ثم إنها وإن كانت بينة في نفسها فلا هي في البيئة إلى غاية لا يرد عليها من الشبه أصلا بل قد يَرِدُ؛ وذلك بوجوه : [أ] منها أن يقال في الضرب الأول إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدًا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان من اللوازم أن يكون ملزوما للشيء ولازما لغيره كذلك. وذلك لا يمكن فيما نحن فيه فإن الاتحاد بين الطرفين في التحقق لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما لكونه متحدا في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ وإنه محال فإن من الصور ما يكون اللازم وهو الأكبر أعم من ملزومه وهو الأوسط، وكذلك الأوسط بالنسبة إلى ملزومه الأصغر، وهذا ظاهر . أو لا يكون لازما ؛ وإنه محال أيضًا وإلا يلزم المحال على تقدير ممكن وهو الافتراق بين الطرفين في الجملة على تقدير كون الأوسط متحدّا، وذلك محال فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم
Then, although they are evident in themselves, they are not so evident that no doubt can be raised against them at all; rather, doubt may occur. This is in several ways: [A] Among them is that it may be said regarding the first mood that it does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is identical [i.e., the same proposition in both premises]. That is subject to denial; for if it were identical, then it would be necessary that it be a consequent of a thing and also a necessary condition for something else. That is not possible in our case because the identity between the two extremes in actual existence cannot but be either a necessary condition for its being identical in every case of these cases; and that is impossible, for among the cases there are those where the necessary condition, which is the major, is more general than its premise, which is the middle, and similarly the middle relative to its premise, the minor, and this is obvious. Or it is not a necessary condition; and that is also impossible, otherwise the impossible would follow from a possible supposition, namely separation between the two extremes in general given the assumption of the middle being identical, and that is impossible, for if it were identical, the major would be among the necessary conditions of ...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the self-evidence of the first figure moods. The objection claims that the first mood only works if the middle term is identical (mutahidan) in both premises. The author refutes this by denying the necessity of identity. He argues that if the middle term were identical, it would have to be both a consequent of one thing and a necessary condition for another, which is not possible given the logical relations. He points out that in some cases, the major (the larger term) is more general than the middle, and the middle is more general than the minor. The argument is not fully preserved, but the author clearly rejects the requirement of identity.

Key Terms
متحد (mutahid): identical, same proposition as middle term in both premises

Key Takeaway
The requirement that the middle term be identical (mutahid) is not necessary for syllogistic validity, as the author refutes by showing impossible consequences.

The Four Self-Evident Moods of First Figure Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates the four productive moods of the first figure conditional syllogism and asserts their self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four moods of the first figure conditional syllogism and produce the correct conclusion from given premises.

Core Idea
The middle term's containment and entailment make first figure moods self-evident.

The author now introduces the four productive moods of the first figure in conditional syllogisms. Unlike the moods of other figures, these are self-evident and require no further proof. Understanding these moods is essential because they form the foundation of valid conditional reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods
Read the passage and list the four moods: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Note how each mood is defined by its premises (major and minor) and conclusion.
The author uses variables: 'أب' for antecedent-consequent pairs, but you can substitute any conditional statements.
2
Analyze the first mood (universal affirmative)
First mood: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D, then E is H' yields 'Whenever A is B, then E is H'. Here the middle term (C is D) contains the minor (A is B) and entails the major (E is H).
3
Analyze the second mood (universal negative)
Second mood: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Not at all if C is D then E is H' yields 'Not at all if A is B then E is H'. The negation in the minor premise negates the consequent entirely.
4
Analyze the third mood (particular affirmative)
Third mood: 'Sometimes if A is B then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D then E is H' yields 'Sometimes if A is B then E is H'. The modality 'sometimes' in the major carries through to the conclusion.
5
Analyze the fourth mood (particular negative)
Fourth mood: 'Sometimes if A is B then C is D' and 'Not at all if C is D then E is H' yields 'Sometimes it is not the case that if A is B then E is H'. The negation of the consequence results in a negative particular conclusion.
The author remarks that these four moods are productive by themselves, unlike those of other figures which need demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضروب الأربعة في الشكل الأول قياسات كاملة بالنسبة إلى المطالب الأربعة وإنتاجها بين بذاتها لما أن الأوسط في كل ضرب منها متضمن للأصغر ومستلزم للأكبر. فالضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان «ج د» فـ «هز» ينتج قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «هز». والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز» . والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» (أب) فـ (ج (د)، وكلما كان (ج) ده فـ هـ ز» ينتج قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (د) هـز». والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان ج) د) فـ هـز» / ينتج قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان أب» فـ «هز»؛ فهذه [۱۸۰ظ] الضروب ستة بنفسها في الإنتاج فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لما يتبين به الصدق في النتائج على خلاف ما يكون من الضروب المنتجة في الأشكال الباقية فإن ذلك من جملة ما له حاجة إلى البيان؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
The four moods in the first figure are perfect syllogisms with respect to the four [possible] demands, and their productivity is self-evident because the middle term in each mood contains the minor and entails the major. So the first mood: our saying 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D, then E is H' produces 'Whenever A is B, then E is H'. The second mood: our saying 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Not at all if C is D then E is H' produces 'Not at all if A is B then E is H'. The third mood: our saying 'Sometimes if A is B then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D then E is H' produces 'Sometimes if A is B then E is H'. The fourth mood: our saying 'Sometimes if A is B then C is D' and 'Not at all if C is D then E is H' produces 'Sometimes it is not the case that if A is B then E is H'. So these six moods (?) are productive by themselves, so there is no need to attend to what shows the truth in the results, contrary to the productive moods in the remaining figures, for that is among what requires demonstration; and that will be known later.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the four productive moods of the first figure in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author begins by stating that these four moods are 'perfect syllogisms' (قياسات كاملة) with self-evident productivity. The reason is that the middle term (الأوسط) contains the minor term (الأصغر) and entails the major term (الأكبر). He then lists each mood with its formal structure and conclusion. The first mood is universal affirmative: if A then B, and if B then C, yields if A then C. The second mood is universal negative: if A then B, and not at all if B then C, yields not at all if A then C. The third mood is particular affirmative: sometimes if A then B, and always if B then C, yields sometimes if A then C. The fourth mood is particular negative: sometimes if A then B, and never if B then C, yields sometimes not if A then C. The author then remarks that these moods are productive by themselves, unlike the moods of other figures which require demonstration. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion of their self-evidence.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb): In this passage, 'darb' refers to a mood of a conditional syllogism, specifying the quantity and quality of the premises. [Note: In earlier texts, 'darb' may refer to any multiplication; here it is technical for syllogistic mood.]
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of conditional syllogisms, where the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard in Aristotelian logic.]
أوسط (awsat): The middle term that connects the minor and major terms in the syllogism. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first mood
B) The second mood
C) The third mood
D) The fourth mood

Key Takeaway
The four self-evident moods of the first figure conditional syllogism are foundational for valid conditional reasoning; they are productive by themselves and do not require additional demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure conditional syllogism in the second mood (universal negative) using a ruling from Islamic jurisprudence. For example, use a premise about prohibition and validity. Identify the middle term and show the conclusion follows necessarily.
?
Given the following argument: 'Whenever an act is riba, it is forbidden. It is not the case that if an act is forbidden, it is permissible. Therefore, whenever an act is riba, it is not permissible.' Determine which mood this corresponds to and verify its validity.

Defending the Unity of the Middle Term
Refutation of objections to unity of middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify objections that challenge the unity of the middle term in first figure syllogisms and reconstruct the author's rebuttal using concepts of necessity and entailment.

Core Idea
The unity of the middle term is not a flaw but the very condition of syllogistic productivity.

In this passage, the author confronts two objections that attack the requirement that the middle term be unified (ittihad) across premises. The first objection claims unity is impossible; the second argues that unity would make the major a necessary condition of the minor, which is false. The author responds by showing that denying unity leads to logical contradictions, thereby defending the foundational role of ittihad in valid inference. By studying these objections, you will learn how to shield essential logical conditions from sophistical attack.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection (أ)
Read objection [أ] carefully. The objector claims that the first mood does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is unified, but that such unity is impossible. He argues that if unified, the major would need to be both a consequence and a condition, which he deems contradictory.
The objection targets the necessary condition (لزوم) of the middle term.
2
Parse the author's rebuttal to (أ)
The author responds that the union of the middle term is precisely what ensures the major is a necessary condition of the minor. He shows that if the major were not a necessary condition, separation between the terms would be possible, contradicting the logical structure. Thus the unity is not a defect but a requirement.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine the second objection (ب)
Objection [ب] directly denies that the middle can be unified at all. The objector argues that if it were, the major would always be a necessary condition of the minor, which is false because the major might depend on the middle without the minor. The author counters by showing that this scenario leads to impossibility: separation between minor and major would still be unavoidable.
Both objections revolve around the relationship of necessity (luzum).
4
Compare the two objections
Both objections attack the same condition (ittihad) from different angles: one argues unity is impossible, the other that it leads to false consequences. The author's rebuttal in each case demonstrates that denying unity produces a greater contradiction, thus affirming the necessity of ittihad for valid inference.
Notice how the author uses logical necessity as a shield.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنها وإن كانت بينة في نفسها فلا هي في البيئة إلى غاية لا يرد عليها من الشبه أصلا بل قد يَرِدُ؛ وذلك بوجوه : [أ] منها أن يقال في الضرب الأول إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدًا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان من اللوازم أن يكون ملزوما للشيء ولازما لغيره كذلك. وذلك لا يمكن فيما نحن فيه فإن الاتحاد بين الطرفين في التحقق لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما لكونه متحدا في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ وإنه محال فإن من الصور ما يكون اللازم وهو الأكبر أعم من ملزومه وهو الأوسط، وكذلك الأوسط بالنسبة إلى ملزومه الأصغر، وهذا ظاهر . أو لا يكون لازما ؛ وإنه محال أيضًا وإلا يلزم المحال على تقدير ممكن وهو الافتراق بين الطرفين في الجملة على تقدير كون الأوسط متحدّا، وذلك محال فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر لزوما كليًّا؛ والافتراق مما ينافيه. [ب] ومنها أن يقال إن الأوسط لا يكون متحدا فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر، وليس كذلك فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم فإما أن يكون الملزوم من الجانبين؛ وذلك محال فإن الأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللزوم من الجانبين، وإما أن لا يكون وإنه محال أيضًا فإن الافتراق بين الأصغر والأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققاً في الجملة أو لا يكون وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللا لزوم من الجانبين. [ت] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم كان الأكبر لازما للأصغر دائما وذلك لا يمكن فإن الأكبر على تقدير عدم الأصغر لا يخلو من أن يكون مساويًا لنقيض ما يقابل أحدهما مطلقا أو لا يكون . فإن لم يكن مساويًا فظاهر فإن من المحال أن يكون الأكبر لازما للأوسط الذي هو لازم للأصغر ولا يكون مساويًا لذلك النقيض. وإن كان مساويًا فكذلك؛ إذ الأصغر لا يمكن أن يكون مساويا للنقيض حينئذ، وإذا لم يكن مساويًا للنقيض لا يكون مساويًا للأكبر . وإنه يحقق الاقتران بينهما في الجملة؛ والافتراق مما ينا في اللزوم دائما. [ث] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدقهما صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم يلزم في جميع الصور، وليس كذلك فإنا إذا [۱۸۱ و] قلنا كلَّما كان الاثنان / فردًا كان عددًا ، وكلَّما كان عددًا كان زوجا فلا يصدق قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا مع صدق كل واحدة من المقدمتين. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية فإنه لا يكون مخصوصا بالضرب الأول. وقد يقال في الضرب الثاني أيضًا إنه لا يكون منتجا وإلا لكان منتجا في مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان متنفسًا، وليس البتة إذا كان متنفسًا كان مستنشقا؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصدق أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان فرسا كان مستنشقا.
Then, although they are self-evident in themselves, they are not in clarity to the extent that no doubt can arise against them at all; rather, doubt may occur. That is in several ways: [A] Among them is that it may be said concerning the first mood that it does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is unified. That is in the domain of prohibition. For if it is unified, it must be such that it is a consequence of something and a necessary condition for something else. That is not possible in our case, because the union between the two terms in actuality is necessarily either a necessary condition for being unified in every one of these forms - and that is impossible, for among the forms there are those in which the necessary condition (i.e., the major) is more general than its consequens (i.e., the middle), and likewise the middle with respect to its consequens the minor, and this is clear - or it is not a necessary condition, and that is also impossible, otherwise an impossibility would follow from a possible assumption, namely the separation between the two terms in general on the assumption that the middle is unified. That is impossible because if it is unified, the major is a universal necessary condition of the minor, and separation contradicts that. [B] Among them is that it may be said that the middle is not unified, because if it were unified, the major would be a necessary condition of the minor, but that is not the case. For if it is a necessary condition, then either the consequens is from both sides - that is impossible, because the major cannot but be either a necessary condition assuming the existence of the middle without the minor, or not; but the entailment should be with entailment from both sides - or it is not, and that is also impossible, because the separation between the minor and the major cannot but be realized in general or not, but the entailment should be with non-entailment from both sides. [C] Among them is that it may be said that the truth of these two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion, because if it did necessitate, the major would always be a necessary condition of the minor, and that is not possible. For the major, on the assumption of the non-existence of the minor, cannot but be either equal to the contradictory of what corresponds to one of them absolutely, or not. If it is not equal, it is obvious, because it is impossible for the major to be a necessary condition of the middle which is a necessary condition of the minor and not be equal to that contradictory. And if it is equal, likewise, because the minor cannot be equal to the contradictory then, and if it is not equal to the contradictory, it is not equal to the major. And that would establish the conjunction between them in general, and separation would contradict the constant entailment. [D] Among them is that it may be said that the truth of the two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion, because if it did necessitate, it would necessitate in all cases, but that is not so. For if we say 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even', then it is not true to say 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' despite the truth of each premise. The same applies to the remaining moods, and it is not specific to the first mood. And it may be said concerning the second mood as well that it is not productive, otherwise it would be productive in an example like: 'Whenever a human is a horse, he is breathing' and 'Not at all if he is breathing, he is sniffing' - and it is not productive, because it is not true to say 'Not at all if he is a horse, he is sniffing'.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four objections (شبه) against the self-evident productivity of the first figure moods. The author structures each objection with a letter [أ], [ب], [ت], [ث] and follows each with a rebuttal. The first objection [أ] argues that the first mood requires the middle term to be 'unified' (متحد) in meaning; the author responds that this unity is inherent and that denying it leads to contradiction. The second objection [ب] attacks the notion that the middle can be unified at all, but the author shows that without such unity the logical relations break down. The third objection [ت] claims that the truth of premises does not guarantee the truth of the conclusion, but the author demonstrates that if the conclusion were false, the premises could not both be true, using the concept of necessary entailment. The fourth objection [ث] provides a counterexample: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' do not yield 'Whenever two is odd, it is even'. However, this is a false analogy because the premises are not universally true; the author indicates that such examples fail to refute the moods. A final remark notes that the second mood is also challenged with an example involving 'human is a horse', but that syllogism fails because the premises are not actually true. Overall, the author systematically defends the first figure moods against these sophistical doubts.

Key Terms
شبه
اتحاد
لزوم

Key Takeaway
The unity of the middle term is not a defect but a necessary condition for syllogistic productivity; denying it leads to logical contradictions.

Refuting Counterexamples to First Figure Moods
Refutation of objections about necessity of conclusion and counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute counterexamples that claim the truth of premises does not guarantee the conclusion in first figure conditional syllogisms, using the author's method of examining the truth of premises.

Core Idea
Counterexamples like 'two is odd' fail because they rely on false premises, not because the syllogistic form is invalid.

The final two objections in this passage attack the very productivity of the first figure. Objection [ت] argues logically that the truth of premises does not necessitate the conclusion, while objection [ث] provides a concrete example with numbers. The author's rebuttal in each case shows that the objector's argument either fails to account for necessary entailment or relies on premises that are not actually true. This lesson will equip you to spot such sophisms and defend valid syllogistic forms.

Guided Path
1
Understand objection [ت]
Objection [ت] claims that the truth of two premises does not guarantee the conclusion, because if it did, the major would always be a necessary condition of the minor, which the objector denies. Read the objection carefully and identify the core logical challenge.
The objector uses the concept of necessity (لزوم) to argue against productivity.
2
Follow the author's rebuttal to [ت]
The author responds by analyzing the possibility of separation between the major and minor. He shows that if the major were not a necessary condition of the minor, then it would be possible for the minor to exist without the major, which contradicts the fact that the minor entails the middle and the middle entails the major. Therefore the major must be a necessary condition.
This is another reductio ad absurdum argument based on the logical chain.
3
Examine the concrete counterexample in [ث]
Objection [ث] provides an actual example: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' do not yield 'Whenever two is odd, it is even', supposedly showing the syllogism fails. Identify the premises and conclusion.
The example is chosen because the premises sound plausible but are false.
4
Identify the flaw in the counterexample
The author notes that the premises are not actually true: 'Two is odd' is false, and 'Every number is even' is also false. Therefore the example does not meet the condition of true premises. A valid counterexample must have true premises but a false conclusion; this one has false premises, so it does not refute the syllogism.
The author also remarks that the same applies to other moods.

ثم إنها وإن كانت بينة في نفسها فلا هي في البيئة إلى غاية لا يرد عليها من الشبه أصلا بل قد يَرِدُ؛ وذلك بوجوه : [أ] منها أن يقال في الضرب الأول إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدًا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان من اللوازم أن يكون ملزوما للشيء ولازما لغيره كذلك. وذلك لا يمكن فيما نحن فيه فإن الاتحاد بين الطرفين في التحقق لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما لكونه متحدا في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ وإنه محال فإن من الصور ما يكون اللازم وهو الأكبر أعم من ملزومه وهو الأوسط، وكذلك الأوسط بالنسبة إلى ملزومه الأصغر، وهذا ظاهر . أو لا يكون لازما ؛ وإنه محال أيضًا وإلا يلزم المحال على تقدير ممكن وهو الافتراق بين الطرفين في الجملة على تقدير كون الأوسط متحدّا، وذلك محال فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر لزوما كليًّا؛ والافتراق مما ينافيه. [ب] ومنها أن يقال إن الأوسط لا يكون متحدا فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر، وليس كذلك فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم فإما أن يكون الملزوم من الجانبين؛ وذلك محال فإن الأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللزوم من الجانبين، وإما أن لا يكون وإنه محال أيضًا فإن الافتراق بين الأصغر والأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققاً في الجملة أو لا يكون وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللا لزوم من الجانبين. [ت] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم كان الأكبر لازما للأصغر دائما وذلك لا يمكن فإن الأكبر على تقدير عدم الأصغر لا يخلو من أن يكون مساويًا لنقيض ما يقابل أحدهما مطلقا أو لا يكون . فإن لم يكن مساويًا فظاهر فإن من المحال أن يكون الأكبر لازما للأوسط الذي هو لازم للأصغر ولا يكون مساويًا لذلك النقيض. وإن كان مساويًا فكذلك؛ إذ الأصغر لا يمكن أن يكون مساويا للنقيض حينئذ، وإذا لم يكن مساويًا للنقيض لا يكون مساويًا للأكبر . وإنه يحقق الاقتران بينهما في الجملة؛ والافتراق مما ينا في اللزوم دائما. [ث] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدقهما صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم يلزم في جميع الصور، وليس كذلك فإنا إذا [۱۸۱ و] قلنا كلَّما كان الاثنان / فردًا كان عددًا ، وكلَّما كان عددًا كان زوجا فلا يصدق قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا مع صدق كل واحدة من المقدمتين. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية فإنه لا يكون مخصوصا بالضرب الأول. وقد يقال في الضرب الثاني أيضًا إنه لا يكون منتجا وإلا لكان منتجا في مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان متنفسًا، وليس البتة إذا كان متنفسًا كان مستنشقا؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصدق أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان فرسا كان مستنشقا.
Then, although they are self-evident in themselves, they are not in clarity to the extent that no doubt can arise against them at all; rather, doubt may occur. That is in several ways: [A] Among them is that it may be said concerning the first mood that it does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is unified. That is in the domain of prohibition. For if it is unified, it must be such that it is a consequence of something and a necessary condition for something else. That is not possible in our case, because the union between the two terms in actuality is necessarily either a necessary condition for being unified in every one of these forms - and that is impossible, for among the forms there are those in which the necessary condition (i.e., the major) is more general than its consequens (i.e., the middle), and likewise the middle with respect to its consequens the minor, and this is clear - or it is not a necessary condition, and that is also impossible, otherwise an impossibility would follow from a possible assumption, namely the separation between the two terms in general on the assumption that the middle is unified. That is impossible because if it is unified, the major is a universal necessary condition of the minor, and separation contradicts that. [B] Among them is that it may be said that the middle is not unified, because if it were unified, the major would be a necessary condition of the minor, but that is not the case. For if it is a necessary condition, then either the consequens is from both sides - that is impossible, because the major cannot but be either a necessary condition assuming the existence of the middle without the minor, or not; but the entailment should be with entailment from both sides - or it is not, and that is also impossible, because the separation between the minor and the major cannot but be realized in general or not, but the entailment should be with non-entailment from both sides. [C] Among them is that it may be said that the truth of these two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion, because if it did necessitate, the major would always be a necessary condition of the minor, and that is not possible. For the major, on the assumption of the non-existence of the minor, cannot but be either equal to the contradictory of what corresponds to one of them absolutely, or not. If it is not equal, it is obvious, because it is impossible for the major to be a necessary condition of the middle which is a necessary condition of the minor and not be equal to that contradictory. And if it is equal, likewise, because the minor cannot be equal to the contradictory then, and if it is not equal to the contradictory, it is not equal to the major. And that would establish the conjunction between them in general, and separation would contradict the constant entailment. [D] Among them is that it may be said that the truth of the two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion, because if it did necessitate, it would necessitate in all cases, but that is not so. For if we say 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even', then it is not true to say 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' despite the truth of each premise. The same applies to the remaining moods, and it is not specific to the first mood. And it may be said concerning the second mood as well that it is not productive, otherwise it would be productive in an example like: 'Whenever a human is a horse, he is breathing' and 'Not at all if he is breathing, he is sniffing' - and it is not productive, because it is not true to say 'Not at all if he is a horse, he is sniffing'.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four objections (شبه) against the self-evident productivity of the first figure moods. The author structures each objection with a letter [أ], [ب], [ت], [ث] and follows each with a rebuttal. The first objection [أ] argues that the first mood requires the middle term to be 'unified' (متحد) in meaning; the author responds that this unity is inherent and that denying it leads to contradiction. The second objection [ب] attacks the notion that the middle can be unified at all, but the author shows that without such unity the logical relations break down. The third objection [ت] claims that the truth of premises does not guarantee the truth of the conclusion, but the author demonstrates that if the conclusion were false, the premises could not both be true, using the concept of necessary entailment. The fourth objection [ث] provides a counterexample: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' do not yield 'Whenever two is odd, it is even'. However, this is a false analogy because the premises are not universally true; the author indicates that such examples fail to refute the moods. A final remark notes that the second mood is also challenged with an example involving 'human is a horse', but that syllogism fails because the premises are not actually true. Overall, the author systematically defends the first figure moods against these sophistical doubts.

A genuine counterexample to a syllogistic form must have true premises and a false conclusion; false premises do not invalidate the form.

Objection and Answer on the Unity of the Middle Term
Author presents an objection against the unity of the middle term and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against the unity of the middle term in a conditional syllogism and analyse the author's refutation using the concept of inclusion.

Core Idea
The middle term is unified not by identity but by inclusion, preserving syllogistic validity.

In this passage, the author anticipates a powerful objection: if the middle term were truly unified, the major term would become a necessary concomitant of the minor, leading to paradox. The objector uses the concepts of implication and separation to challenge the very foundation of conditional syllogisms. The author's response reframes 'unity' as inclusion, where the middle term encompasses the minor term. By following this dialectical exchange, you will sharpen your ability to defend logical structures against sophisticated critiques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's core claim
The objector argues that the middle term cannot be unified because if it were, the major term would be a necessary concomitant of the minor, leading to contradictions about implication from both sides or lack thereof.
2
Examine the objector's use of implication and separation
The objector sets up a dilemma: either the major is a concomitant from both sides (impossible) or not (also impossible), and similarly with separation between minor and major.
3
Locate the author's response strategy
The author reinterprets unity: the middle term encompasses the minor term, so the minor is included in the major premise. This makes the middle term 'unified' in the sense of being constant across premises.
4
Test the author's illustration with variables
The author translates the statement into a form with variables (A, B, C, D, H, Z) to show that the middle term (C D) remains the same in both premises, just as it appears in the minor premise.
5
Assess the refutation of the doubt
The author concludes that separation on the assumption of union does not necessarily negate implication, because implication can hold conditionally even if the two extremes are not necessarily connected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] ومنها أن يقال إن الأوسط لا يكون متحدا فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر، وليس كذلك فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم فإما أن يكون الملزوم من الجانبين؛ وذلك محال فإن الأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللزوم من الجانبين، وإما أن لا يكون وإنه محال أيضًا فإن الافتراق بين الأصغر والأكبر لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققاً في الجملة أو لا يكون وإنما كان ينبغي اللزوم مع اللا لزوم من الجانبين. أما الجواب فنقول [أ] في الأول إن الأوسط متحدا لما مر من قبل أن الأوسط متضمن للأصغر فكان الأصغر مندرجا في الكبرى فكان قولنا كلما كان جده في ( هـ ز) بمنزلة قولنا كلما كان «أب» وغير «أب» من الأشياء المستلزمة لـ «ج د» فـ «هز»، فالأوسط إذن وهو (ج (د) كما يكون ثابتا في الصغرى يكون ثابتا في الكبرى؛ وهو المعنى من كون الأوسط متحدا. وأما ما ذكرتم من الشبهة فذلك لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الافتراق في الجملة على ذلك التقدير محالا، وليس كذلك فإن الافتراق على ذلك التقدير من اللوازم إذا لم يكن الاتحاد بين الطرفين لازما وإلا لكان الاتحاد وهو اللزوم من الجانبين لازما على تقدير أن لا يكون لازما.
[B] Among them is that it is said that the middle term is not unified; because if it were unified, then the major term would be among the necessary concomitants of the minor term, but it is not so. For if it is among the concomitants, then either the necessary concomitant is from both sides—and that is impossible, for the major term cannot but be either a necessary concomitant on the assumption of the realization of the middle term without the minor term, or not—whereas what was required is implication together with implication from both sides; or it is not, and that is also impossible, for the separation between the minor and major terms cannot but be either actually realized in general or not, whereas what was required is implication together with non-implication from both sides. As for the answer, we say [A] regarding the first: the middle term is unified for what has passed: because the middle term encompasses the minor term, so the minor term is included in the major premise. Thus our statement 'Whenever it is [such that] its limit is in (H Z)' is like our statement 'Whenever it is [such that] A is B, and other than A B of the things that necessitate C D, then H Z.' So the middle term, which is (C (D), just as it is established in the minor premise, is established in the major premise; and that is the meaning of the middle term being unified. As for the doubt you mentioned, that is not valid unless separation in general is impossible on that assumption, which is not the case. For separation on that assumption is among the concomitants if the union between the two extremes is not necessary; otherwise, union—which is implication from both sides—would be necessary on the assumption that it is not necessary.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection followed by an answer. The objection argues that the middle term cannot be unified in conditional syllogisms because if it were, the major term would be a necessary concomitant of the minor term, which leads to contradictions regarding implication and separation. The author responds by clarifying that the middle term is unified in the sense that it encompasses the minor term, so the minor term is included in the major premise. He illustrates with variables: the statement 'Whenever it is such that its limit is in (H Z)' is equivalent to 'Whenever it is such that A is B and other things that necessitate C D, then H Z,' showing that the middle term remains constant across premises. The author then refutes the doubt by explaining that separation on the assumption of union does not necessarily contradict implication, as the implication may still hold conditionally.

اللزوم
الافتراق
الاتحاد

Key Takeaway
The unity of the middle term in conditional syllogisms is a matter of inclusion, not identity, allowing the syllogism to remain valid despite challenges about implication and separation.
