Objection and Refutation on Premises Entailing Conclusion
Author presents an objection and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection against syllogistic necessity and reconstruct the author's refutation using the concepts of concomitant and generality of terms.

Core Idea
True premises entail conclusion only if the major is always concomitant of the minor—but the objector's proof fails due to term generality.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the fundamental principle that true syllogistic premises guarantee a true conclusion. The objector argues that the major term would have to be a necessary concomitant of the minor in all cases, which is impossible. However, the author demonstrates that the objector's reasoning rests on an unwarranted assumption about the relationship between the major, middle, and minor terms. By understanding this exchange, you will learn how dialectical precision protects the foundation of logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Locate the objection's main thesis
Find the sentence where the objector states that truth of premises does not necessitate truth of conclusion. The key phrase is 'لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة'.
This is the challenge to the fundamental principle of syllogistic validity.
2
Analyze the objector's conditional reasoning
The objector argues: if the conclusion were necessary, then the major term would always be a necessary concomitant of the minor. He then shows this is impossible using the concept of 'musāwin' (equal) with the contradictory.
Focus on the part beginning with 'فإنه إذا لزم كان الأكبر لازما للأصغر دائما'.
3
Identify the author's refutation strategy
The author responds by questioning the objector's assumption that the major must be a concomitant of the middle. He introduces the possibility that the middle term is more general than the minor, so the major may be a concomitant of the minor even if not of the middle in that scenario.
Find the phrase 'نقول لمَ قُلتم لأن الأكبر إذا لم يكن لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر لا يكون الأكبر لازما للأصغر بل يمكن أن يكون لازما'.
4
Assess the outcome of the dialectical exchange
Determine whether the author successfully refutes the objection. The author shows that the objector's argument does not hold because it ignores the case where the middle is more general, thus preserving the validity of the inference.
The author's response effectively restores the principle that true premises necessitate the conclusion in a valid syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم كان الأكبر لازما للأصغر دائما وذلك لا يمكن فإن الأكبر على تقدير عدم الأصغر لا يخلو من أن يكون مساويًا لنقيض ما يقابل أحدهما مطلقا أو لا يكون . فإن لم يكن مساويًا فظاهر فإن من المحال أن يكون الأكبر لازما للأوسط الذي هو لازم للأصغر ولا يكون مساويًا لذلك النقيض. وإن كان مساويًا فكذلك؛ إذ الأصغر لا يمكن أن يكون مساويا للنقيض حينئذ، وإذا لم يكن مساويًا للنقيض لا يكون مساويًا للأكبر . وإنه يحقق الاقتران بينهما في الجملة؛ والافتراق مما ينا في اللزوم دائما. [ب] وأما في الثاني فنقول لمَ قُلتم لأن الأكبر إذا لم يكن لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر لا يكون الأكبر لازما للأصغر بل يمكن أن يكون لازما ؛ وذلك اللزوم من الجانبين أيضًا وإلا لكان لازما للأوسط على القدير كونه لازما للأصغر لا محالة ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك ؛ وقد كان من الجائز أن يكون الأوسط أعم من الأصغر أو نقول إنه لازم للأصغر حينئذ. فإن من المحال أن لا يكون لازما لا للأصغر ولا للأوسط في نفس الأمر، وقد كان لازما لكل واحد منهما أو لأحدهما وهو الأوسط في نفس الأمر؛ وإنه من جملة ما تحقق كونه لازما للأصغر على تقدير عدم كونه لازما للأوسط.
[T] Among them is that it is said that the truth of these two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion; because if it did necessitate, then the major term would always be a necessary concomitant of the minor, and that is impossible. For the major term, on the assumption of the absence of the minor, cannot but be either equal to the contradictory of what corresponds to either of the two absolutely or not. If it is not equal, then it is obvious that it is impossible for the major to be a necessary concomitant of the middle, which is a concomitant of the minor, without being equal to that contradictory. And if it is equal, it is likewise: for the minor cannot then be equal to the contradictory, and if it is not equal to the contradictory, it is not equal to the major. Thus it establishes the conjunction between them in general; and separation is contrary to implication always. [B] As for the second, we say: why did you say that because the major, if it is not a necessary concomitant on the assumption of the realization of the middle without the minor, then the major is not a necessary concomitant of the minor? Rather, it is possible that it is a concomitant; and that implication is from both sides as well, otherwise it would be a concomitant of the middle on the assumption that it is a concomitant of the minor necessarily, and it does not have to be so. It is possible that the middle is more general than the minor, or we say that it is then a concomitant of the minor. For it is impossible for it not to be a concomitant of either the minor or the middle in reality, and it was a concomitant of each of them or of one of them—namely the middle—in reality; and it is among the things that are realized as being a concomitant of the minor on the assumption of its not being a concomitant of the middle.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical sequence. The objection argues that true premises do not guarantee a true conclusion because if they did, the major term would always be a necessary concomitant of the minor, which is false. The objector uses a case where the major term might be equal to the contradictory of something, leading to contradictions that prevent the inference. The author responds by challenging the objector's assumption: even if the major is not a concomitant of the middle on a certain assumption, it may still be a concomitant of the minor. The author points out that the middle term might be more general than the minor, so the implication from the middle to the major does not need to hold for the conclusion to follow. In fact, the major can be a concomitant of the minor even if it is not a concomitant of the middle in that scenario.

المقدمتين
النتيجة
مساوٍ

B) The middle term may be more general than the minor, so the implication from the middle is not required.
C) The premises are not actually true in the objection's example.
D) The conclusion must be false if the premises are true.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires that the conclusion follows from premises; the objector's attempt to deny this by arguing against necessary concomitance fails when the possibility of a more general middle term is considered.

The Objection to Conditional Syllogisms
Objector presents two counterexamples to challenge the productivity of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's counterexamples against conditional syllogisms and identify the logical structure of his objection.

Core Idea
A single counterexample with false antecedent does not disprove a conditional syllogism.

The objector attempts to show that conditional syllogisms do not always yield true conclusions by presenting two concrete examples. Let's examine the structure of his argument and the examples he provides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the truth of the two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion. This is a direct challenge to the validity of conditional syllogisms.
2
Extract the first conditional syllogism
The objector uses: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number; whenever it is a number, it is even; therefore whenever two is odd, it is even.' Identify the antecedent (P: two is odd), middle (Q: it is a number), and consequent (R: it is even). The conclusion is that if P then R.
The premises are conditional propositions themselves.
3
Analyze why the objector thinks it fails
The objector notes that the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' is false because two is not odd, yet the premises seem true. He assumes that truth of premises should guarantee truth of conclusion in all cases.
He neglects the fact that conditional statements can be true even when the antecedent is false.
4
Examine the second example
The objector gives: 'Whenever a human is a horse, it is a breathing being; whenever it is a breathing being, it is a snoring being; therefore whenever a human is a horse, it is a snoring being.' Again, the conclusion is false (since a human is never a horse), but premises are conditionally true.
This example is meant to show the same pattern applies to the second mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ث] ومنها أن يقال لا يلزم من صدقهما صدق النتيجة فإنه إذا لزم يلزم في جميع الصور، وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الاثنان فردًا كان عددًا، وكلَّما كان عددًا كان زوجا فلا يصدق قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا مع صدق كل واحدة من المقدمتين. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية فإنه لا يكون مخصوصا بالضرب الأول. وقد يقال في الضرب الثاني أيضًا إنه لا يكون منتجا وإلا لكان منتجا في مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان متنفسًا، وليس البتة إذا كان متنفسًا كان مستنشقا؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصدق أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان فرسا كان مستنشقا. [ت] وأما في الثالث فنقول هب أن الافتراق لازم بينهما على تقدير أن لا يكون هذا مساويًا لذلك فلمَ قُلتم بأن الافتراق بينهما مما ينافي اللزوم دائما بل لا ينافيه. فإن اللزوم الدائمة عبارة عما يكون متحققاً على جميع التقادير؛ وهذا التقدير وهو تقدير الافتراق واحد من جميع التقادير. ولا يظن أن الشيء إذا كان لازما للشيء فذلك لا يكون لازما لذلك الشيء على تقدير تحقق ما ينافي كونه لازما لذلك الشيء بل يكون لازما على هذا التقدير أيضًا حتى إذا تحقق أو فرض.
[Th] Among them is that it is said that the truth of the two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion; because if it necessitated, it would necessitate in all forms, but it is not so. For we say: Whenever two is odd, it is a number; and whenever it is a number, it is even; then our statement 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' is not true, despite each premise being true. And likewise for the remaining moods, for it is not specific to the first mood. It is also said regarding the second mood that it is not productive; otherwise it would be productive in an example like our saying: Whenever a human is a horse, it is a breathing being; and it is not at all true that whenever it is a breathing being, it is a snoring being; so it is not productive, for it is not true to say 'Not at all: whenever it is a horse, it is a snoring being.' [T] As for the third, we say: suppose separation is necessary between them on the assumption that this is not equal to that; then why did you say that separation between them is contrary to implication always? Rather, it does not contradict it. For perpetual implication is that which is realized in all situations; and this situation—namely the situation of separation—is one of all situations. And one should not think that if a thing is a necessary concomitant of another, then it is not a necessary concomitant of that thing on the assumption of the realization of what contradicts its being a necessary concomitant of that thing; rather, it is a necessary concomitant on this assumption as well, even if it is realized or assumed.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage contains a third objection and its answer. The objection provides concrete examples: first, a conditional syllogism with 'two is odd' as antecedent leading to 'it is even' through the middle 'it is a number', but the conclusion is false because two is not odd, so the example shows that true premises (the conditionals) do not yield a true conclusion in that specific case. The objector generalizes that this holds for all moods, not just the first. A second example: 'Whenever a human is a horse, it is a breathing being; and whenever it is a breathing being, it is a snoring being' leads to 'Whenever a human is a horse, it is a snoring being' which is not true, showing the second mood is also unproductive. The author answers by distinguishing between perpetual implication and implication under specific assumptions. He argues that separation in one scenario does not negate the implication if it still holds in other scenarios. Perpetual implication means it is realized in all situations, and the situation of separation is just one of many; the implication holds even under that assumption. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
الضرب الأول (al-ḍarb al-awwal): The first mood or figure of conditional syllogisms, which the objector claims is not uniquely productive. [Note: In categorical syllogisms, 'ḍarb' refers to a mood of a figure; here it refers to a mood in conditional syllogisms.]
اللزوم الدائمة (al-luzūm al-dā'ima): Perpetual or absolute implication that holds in all situations without exception. [Note: This is distinguished from conditional implication; a standard concept in logic.]
التقادير (al-taqādīr): Situations or assumptions under which the truth of the implications is considered. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The objector uses the example 'two is odd' in order to demonstrate that:
A) conditional syllogisms are always valid.
B) the conclusion does not follow even if premises are true.
C) the premises are false.
D) the first mood is not productive.

Key Takeaway
The objector's challenge assumes that truth of premises should guarantee truth of conclusion in all cases, but the example uses a false antecedent, which does not affect the conditional truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. The objector uses the example 'two is odd' to challenge the first mood. Write out the conditional syllogism in the form 'If P then Q, If Q then R, therefore If P then R.' Then explain why the objector thinks the conclusion is false. (Hint: consider the truth value of P in real world.)

2. Find a similar potential counterexample from Islamic theology (e.g., 'If someone is a prophet, they are infallible; if infallible, they do not err; therefore if someone is a prophet, they do not err.') and discuss why it does not actually refute the syllogism.

The Author's Refutation: Perpetual Implication
Author refutes the objection using the concept of perpetual implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's concept of perpetual implication and how it refutes the objector's examples.

Core Idea
Perpetual implication holds in all situations, including those where the antecedent is false.

The author responds to the objection by clarifying the nature of perpetual implication. He argues that implication is not invalidated by a scenario where the antecedent is false, because perpetual implication must hold in every scenario, including that one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's key distinction
The author distinguishes between perpetual implication (luzūm dā'im) and implication under specific assumptions. Perpetual implication is that which is realized in all situations (taqādīr).
2
Apply to the objector's examples
For the example 'two is odd', the implication 'if two is odd then it is even' is a perpetual implication. Even though in reality two is not odd, the conditional statement is true because material implication is true when antecedent false. The author says the implication holds 'on this assumption as well'.
The objector incorrectly assumed that the truth of the premises requires the antecedent to be true in the actual world.
3
Analyze the author's argument about separation
The author says that separation (of antecedent and consequent) in one scenario does not negate perpetual implication. He uses the third mood example to argue that even if the antecedent is false (separation), the implication still holds because it is considered under all possible assumptions.
4
Evaluate the refutation
The author's refutation is effective: it shows that the objector's examples rely on a misunderstanding of conditional truth. By defining perpetual implication as holding in all situations, the objector's 'counterexamples' are not actually violations.
This defense preserves the validity of conditional syllogisms.

The Syllogism

The Unity of the Middle Term in Conditional Syllogisms
Author refutes four criticisms regarding the unity of the middle term in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in a conditional syllogism and verify its unity by checking if it is subsumed under both premises.

Core Idea
The middle term must be unified across premises for the conditional syllogism to connect antecedent and consequent.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī), the middle term (al-awsat) plays a crucial role in linking the antecedent of the minor premise to the consequent of the major. But objectors have questioned whether the middle term truly remains the same in both premises. In this passage, the author refutes the first objection by clarifying what unity of the middle term means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in the conditional syllogism
Look for the term that appears in both the minor and major premises. In the abstract example, it is (J D).
The middle term may be a complex condition, not just a single word.
2
Check subsumption under both premises
The minor premise contains the middle term and the minor term; the major premise contains the middle term and the major term. The author says the middle term includes the minor term, so the minor is subsumed under the major.
3
Refute the doubt about separation
The objector claims that if the middle and minor can separate, unity fails. The author argues that separation is possible but does not invalidate implication.
The union is not necessary for the syllogism to be valid.
4
Apply to a real Islamic example
Take a conditional syllogism like: 'If the imam is just, then his rule is legitimate. If this imam is just...' Identify the middle term (justice of the imam) and see if it links the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجواب فنقول [أ] في الأول إن الأوسط متحدا لما مر من قبل أن الأوسط متضمن للأصغر فكان الأصغر مندرجا في الكبرى فكان قولنا كلما كان جده في ( هـ ز) بمنزلة قولنا كلما كان «أب» وغير «أب» من الأشياء المستلزمة لـ «ج د» فـ «هز»، فالأوسط إذن وهو (ج (د) كما يكون ثابتا في الصغرى يكون ثابتا في الكبرى؛ وهو المعنى من كون الأوسط متحدا. وأما ما ذكرتم من الشبهة فذلك لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الافتراق في الجملة على ذلك التقدير محالا، وليس كذلك فإن الافتراق على ذلك التقدير من اللوازم إذا لم يكن الاتحاد بين الطرفين لازما وإلا لكان الاتحاد وهو اللزوم من الجانبين لازما على تقدير أن لا يكون لازما . [ب] وأما في الثاني فنقول لمَ قُلتم لأن الأكبر إذا لم يكن لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر لا يكون الأكبر لازما للأصغر بل يمكن أن يكون لازما ؛ وذلك اللزوم من الجانبين أيضًا وإلا لكان لازما للأوسط على القدير كونه لازما للأصغر لا محالة ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك ؛ وقد كان من الجائز أن يكون الأوسط أعم من الأصغر أو نقول إنه لازم للأصغر حينئذ. فإن من المحال أن لا يكون لازما لا للأصغر ولا للأوسط في نفس الأمر، وقد كان لازما لكل واحد منهما أو لأحدهما وهو الأوسط في نفس الأمر؛ وإنه من جملة ما تحقق كونه لازما للأصغر على تقدير عدم كونه لازما للأوسط. [ت] وأما في الثالث فنقول هب أن الافتراق لازم بينهما على تقدير أن لا يكون هذا مساويًا لذلك فلمَ قُلتم بأن الافتراق بينهما مما ينافي اللزوم دائما بل لا ينافيه. فإن اللزوم الدائمة عبارة عما يكون متحققاً على جميع التقادير؛ وهذا التقدير وهو تقدير الافتراق واحد من جميع التقادير. ولا يظن أن الشيء إذا كان لازما للشيء فذلك لا يكون لازما لذلك الشيء على تقدير تحقق ما ينافي كونه لازما لذلك الشيء بل يكون لازما على هذا التقدير أيضًا حتى إذا تحقق أو فرض ملزومه على هذا التقدير وجب أن يتحقق لازمه وإلا لا يكون لازما؛ وهذا من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه. [ث] وأما في الرابع وهو الشبهة المشهورة فنقول قد مرَّ من قبل أن مثل هذا القياس ينتج فإن المحقق لهذه القضية وهي كلَّما كانت الاثنان فردًا عددًا إذا وضعها على أنها حق وكان حقا في نفسه أنه إذا كان عددًا كان زوجا يلزم ذلك المحقق أنه إذا كان فردًا كان زوجا لما أنه يجمع بين هاتين المقدمتين؛ ويُسلم أن كل واحدة منهما حق، والقياس ينتج بحسب التسليم على ما عرف ومنهم من أجاب عنه وقال إن الكبرى في حيز المنع إذا كانت لزومية (١)، وذلك لأنه لا يلزم من فرض كونه عددًا في جميع الأحوال أن يكون زوجا؛ وقد كان من جملة تلك الأحوال أن يكون فردًا. غير أنه فاسد لما أن الزوجية للاثنين بحسب الذات، وفرض كونه عددًا لا يخرجه عن كونه اثنين بل فرض كونه فردًا لا يخرجه؛ فكان اثنين إذن كلما كان عددًا، وكان زوجًا كذلك وإلا لا يكون اثنين في جميع الأحوال؛ وذلك لا يمكن؛ إذ الكلام في الاثنين. وأما قوله في الضرب الثاني فذلك لا ينتج لما أنَّ الكبرى فيه لا تكون صادقة؛ وقد كان الإنسان متصفًا بصفة كونه متنفسا دائما، وبصفة كونه مستنشقا كذلك. (١) النسخة- إذا كانت لزومية، صح هامش.
As for the answer, we say: [A] Regarding the first: The middle term is unified because, as previously stated, the middle term includes the minor term, so the minor term is subsumed under the major premise. Thus, our statement 'Whenever its grandfather in (H Z)' is analogous to our statement 'Whenever 'AB' and other than 'AB' among the things entailing 'J D', so 'H Z''. Hence the middle term, namely (J D), just as it is established in the minor premise, is also established in the major premise; that is the meaning of the middle term being unified. As for the doubt you mentioned, that is not complete unless separation in the whole is impossible on that assumption, but it is not so; for separation on that assumption is among the necessary concomitants if the union between the two sides is not necessary, otherwise the union—which is the implication from both sides—would be necessary on the assumption that it is not necessary. [B] Regarding the second, we say: Why did you say that if the major term is not a consequent on the assumption of the middle term being realized without the minor term, then the major term is not a consequent of the minor term? Rather, it can be a consequent; and that implication from both sides as well, otherwise it would be a consequent of the middle term on the assumption of it being a consequent of the minor term necessarily, and it is not necessary that it be so. It is possible that the middle term is more general than the minor term, or we say that it is a consequent of the minor term at that time. For it is impossible that it is not a consequent of either the minor or the middle in actual fact, and it was a consequent of each of them or one of them—namely the middle—in actual fact; and it is among the things that are realized as being a consequent of the minor term on the assumption of it not being a consequent of the middle. [C] Regarding the third, we say: Suppose that separation between them is necessary on the assumption that this one is not equal to that one; why did you say that separation between them contradicts implication always? Rather, it does not contradict it. For perpetual implication is that which is realized under all assumptions; and this assumption—the assumption of separation—is one of all assumptions. And do not think that if something is a consequent of a thing, then it is not a consequent of that thing under the assumption of the realization of something that contradicts its being a consequent of that thing; rather, it is a consequent under this assumption as well, such that if its antecedents is realized or assumed under this assumption, its consequent must be realized; otherwise it is not a consequent. This is among what has been discussed before. [D] Regarding the fourth, which is the famous doubt, we say: It has passed before that such a syllogism yields a conclusion. For the one who establishes this proposition—namely 'Whenever two is an odd number'—if he posits it as true, and it is true in itself that 'if it is a number, it is even'—then it follows for that establisher that 'if it is odd, it is even' because he combines these two premises; and it is conceded that each of them is true, and the syllogism yields a conclusion according to the concession, as known. Some answered it saying that the major premise is subject to rejection if it is implicative (luzumiyya), because it does not follow from assuming it to be a number in all circumstances that it is even; and among those circumstances is that it be odd. But this is invalid because evenness belongs to two essentially, and assuming it to be a number does not take it out of being two; rather, assuming it to be odd does not take it out. So it is two whenever it is a number, and it is even likewise; otherwise it would not be two in all circumstances, and that is impossible, because the discussion is about two. As for his statement about the second mood, it does not yield a conclusion because the major premise in it is not true; a human being is described with the attribute of being a breather always, and with the attribute of being an inhaler likewise.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of four objections (listed as [أ], [ب], [ت], [ث]) concerning conditional syllogisms, specifically about the necessity of the middle term's unity and the conditions for necessary implication. The author follows the order of the objections, each introduced with a response.

In [أ], the author first establishes that the middle term is 'united' (muttaḥid) in the sense that it is subsumed under both the minor and major premises. The middle term (e.g., 'J D') is contained in the minor and also appears in the major, ensuring the link. He refutes a doubt that separation (iftirāq) between the middle and the minor is impossible; rather, separation can occur if the union is not necessary, but the implication from both sides must hold on all assumptions.

In [ب], the objection is that if the major term is not entailed when the middle is realized without the minor, then it cannot be entailed by the minor. The author responds by noting that the major could still be entailed by the minor, especially if the middle is more general than the minor. He argues that it is impossible for the major to not be entailed by either the minor or the middle in reality; thus, the entailment still holds.

In [ت], the doubt is that separation between the two sides negates perpetual implication. The author clarifies that perpetual implication (al-luzūm al-dā'ima) means the implication holds under all assumptions, including the assumption of separation. Even if separation is assumed, the consequent must still follow from the antecedent when the antecedent is realized.

In [ث], the famous doubt (al-shubha al-mashhūra) is addressed. The example given is a conditional syllogism: 'Whenever two is an odd number; if it is a number, it is even.' The objection says the major premise 'if it is a number, it is even' is false when the number is odd. The author refutes this by noting that 'two' essentially has evenness; regardless of being assumed odd, it remains two and therefore even. This is similar to saying that if a thing is a revealed book (like the Qur'an), it is essentially from God, even if we assume it is not revealed. The author also mentions a second mood that is not productive because the major premise is not universally true; an example is 'human is always a breather and an inhaler', but these are not essentially linked.

The key principle here is that for a conditional syllogism to be valid, the major premise must be a necessary implication (luzūm) that holds in all circumstances, grounded in the essence of the subject.

Key Terms
أوسط
لزوم
شبهة
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does it mean for the middle term to be unified?
A) It is the same word in both premises.
B) It is subsumed under both premises and links them.
C) It cannot be separated from the minor term.
D) It must be a necessary condition for the premise's truth.

Key Takeaway
The middle term's unity means it is subsumed under both premises, enabling the conditional syllogism to connect antecedent and consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism about the proof of God's existence where the middle term is 'temporal origination' and verify its unity.

Implication and Separation: Refuting the Second and Third Objections
Author refutes four criticisms regarding the unity of the middle term in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the validity of conditional syllogisms against objections regarding separation of terms and necessary implication.

Core Idea
Necessary implication (luzūm) holds even when terms can be separated conceptually.

Objectors argue that if the major term is not entailed when the middle term exists without the minor, or if separation between terms is possible, then the implication fails. The author counters that implication can still hold because the major term may be entailed by the minor directly, and perpetual implication means it holds under all assumptions including separation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
The claim: if the major is not a consequent of the middle alone, it cannot be a consequent of the minor. The author says it can be, because the middle may be more general.
2
Follow the author's response
He argues that even if the middle is more general, the major could still be entailed by the minor. In actual fact, the major is entailed by either the minor or the middle.
3
Grasp the third objection
The claim: if separation between the antecedent and consequent is possible, then implication cannot be perpetual. The author responds that perpetual implication means it holds even under the assumption of separation.
4
Apply to kalam
For example, in the argument 'If the world is created, it has a Creator. If it is a substance, it is created.' The implication 'if substance then created' holds even if we assume the world is not a substance (separation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجواب فنقول [أ] في الأول إن الأوسط متحدا لما مر من قبل أن الأوسط متضمن للأصغر فكان الأصغر مندرجا في الكبرى فكان قولنا كلما كان جده في ( هـ ز) بمنزلة قولنا كلما كان «أب» وغير «أب» من الأشياء المستلزمة لـ «ج د» فـ «هز»، فالأوسط إذن وهو (ج (د) كما يكون ثابتا في الصغرى يكون ثابتا في الكبرى؛ وهو المعنى من كون الأوسط متحدا. وأما ما ذكرتم من الشبهة فذلك لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الافتراق في الجملة على ذلك التقدير محالا، وليس كذلك فإن الافتراق على ذلك التقدير من اللوازم إذا لم يكن الاتحاد بين الطرفين لازما وإلا لكان الاتحاد وهو اللزوم من الجانبين لازما على تقدير أن لا يكون لازما . [ب] وأما في الثاني فنقول لمَ قُلتم لأن الأكبر إذا لم يكن لازما على تقدير تحقق الأوسط دون الأصغر لا يكون الأكبر لازما للأصغر بل يمكن أن يكون لازما ؛ وذلك اللزوم من الجانبين أيضًا وإلا لكان لازما للأوسط على القدير كونه لازما للأصغر لا محالة ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك ؛ وقد كان من الجائز أن يكون الأوسط أعم من الأصغر أو نقول إنه لازم للأصغر حينئذ. فإن من المحال أن لا يكون لازما لا للأصغر ولا للأوسط في نفس الأمر، وقد كان لازما لكل واحد منهما أو لأحدهما وهو الأوسط في نفس الأمر؛ وإنه من جملة ما تحقق كونه لازما للأصغر على تقدير عدم كونه لازما للأوسط. [ت] وأما في الثالث فنقول هب أن الافتراق لازم بينهما على تقدير أن لا يكون هذا مساويًا لذلك فلمَ قُلتم بأن الافتراق بينهما مما ينافي اللزوم دائما بل لا ينافيه. فإن اللزوم الدائمة عبارة عما يكون متحققاً على جميع التقادير؛ وهذا التقدير وهو تقدير الافتراق واحد من جميع التقادير. ولا يظن أن الشيء إذا كان لازما للشيء فذلك لا يكون لازما لذلك الشيء على تقدير تحقق ما ينافي كونه لازما لذلك الشيء بل يكون لازما على هذا التقدير أيضًا حتى إذا تحقق أو فرض ملزومه على هذا التقدير وجب أن يتحقق لازمه وإلا لا يكون لازما؛ وهذا من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه. [ث] وأما في الرابع وهو الشبهة المشهورة فنقول قد مرَّ من قبل أن مثل هذا القياس ينتج فإن المحقق لهذه القضية وهي كلَّما كانت الاثنان فردًا عددًا إذا وضعها على أنها حق وكان حقا في نفسه أنه إذا كان عددًا كان زوجا يلزم ذلك المحقق أنه إذا كان فردًا كان زوجا لما أنه يجمع بين هاتين المقدمتين؛ ويُسلم أن كل واحدة منهما حق، والقياس ينتج بحسب التسليم على ما عرف ومنهم من أجاب عنه وقال إن الكبرى في حيز المنع إذا كانت لزومية (١)، وذلك لأنه لا يلزم من فرض كونه عددًا في جميع الأحوال أن يكون زوجا؛ وقد كان من جملة تلك الأحوال أن يكون فردًا. غير أنه فاسد لما أن الزوجية للاثنين بحسب الذات، وفرض كونه عددًا لا يخرجه عن كونه اثنين بل فرض كونه فردًا لا يخرجه؛ فكان اثنين إذن كلما كان عددًا، وكان زوجًا كذلك وإلا لا يكون اثنين في جميع الأحوال؛ وذلك لا يمكن؛ إذ الكلام في الاثنين. وأما قوله في الضرب الثاني فذلك لا ينتج لما أنَّ الكبرى فيه لا تكون صادقة؛ وقد كان الإنسان متصفًا بصفة كونه متنفسا دائما، وبصفة كونه مستنشقا كذلك. (١) النسخة- إذا كانت لزومية، صح هامش.
As for the answer, we say: [A] Regarding the first: The middle term is unified because, as previously stated, the middle term includes the minor term, so the minor term is subsumed under the major premise. Thus, our statement 'Whenever its grandfather in (H Z)' is analogous to our statement 'Whenever 'AB' and other than 'AB' among the things entailing 'J D', so 'H Z''. Hence the middle term, namely (J D), just as it is established in the minor premise, is also established in the major premise; that is the meaning of the middle term being unified. As for the doubt you mentioned, that is not complete unless separation in the whole is impossible on that assumption, but it is not so; for separation on that assumption is among the necessary concomitants if the union between the two sides is not necessary, otherwise the union—which is the implication from both sides—would be necessary on the assumption that it is not necessary. [B] Regarding the second, we say: Why did you say that if the major term is not a consequent on the assumption of the middle term being realized without the minor term, then the major term is not a consequent of the minor term? Rather, it can be a consequent; and that implication from both sides as well, otherwise it would be a consequent of the middle term on the assumption of it being a consequent of the minor term necessarily, and it is not necessary that it be so. It is possible that the middle term is more general than the minor term, or we say that it is a consequent of the minor term at that time. For it is impossible that it is not a consequent of either the minor or the middle in actual fact, and it was a consequent of each of them or one of them—namely the middle—in actual fact; and it is among the things that are realized as being a consequent of the minor term on the assumption of it not being a consequent of the middle. [C] Regarding the third, we say: Suppose that separation between them is necessary on the assumption that this one is not equal to that one; why did you say that separation between them contradicts implication always? Rather, it does not contradict it. For perpetual implication is that which is realized under all assumptions; and this assumption—the assumption of separation—is one of all assumptions. And do not think that if something is a consequent of a thing, then it is not a consequent of that thing under the assumption of the realization of something that contradicts its being a consequent of that thing; rather, it is a consequent under this assumption as well, such that if its antecedents is realized or assumed under this assumption, its consequent must be realized; otherwise it is not a consequent. This is among what has been discussed before. [D] Regarding the fourth, which is the famous doubt, we say: It has passed before that such a syllogism yields a conclusion. For the one who establishes this proposition—namely 'Whenever two is an odd number'—if he posits it as true, and it is true in itself that 'if it is a number, it is even'—then it follows for that establisher that 'if it is odd, it is even' because he combines these two premises; and it is conceded that each of them is true, and the syllogism yields a conclusion according to the concession, as known. Some answered it saying that the major premise is subject to rejection if it is implicative (luzumiyya), because it does not follow from assuming it to be a number in all circumstances that it is even; and among those circumstances is that it be odd. But this is invalid because evenness belongs to two essentially, and assuming it to be a number does not take it out of being two; rather, assuming it to be odd does not take it out. So it is two whenever it is a number, and it is even likewise; otherwise it would not be two in all circumstances, and that is impossible, because the discussion is about two. As for his statement about the second mood, it does not yield a conclusion because the major premise in it is not true; a human being is described with the attribute of being a breather always, and with the attribute of being an inhaler likewise.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of four objections (listed as [أ], [ب], [ت], [ث]) concerning conditional syllogisms, specifically about the necessity of the middle term's unity and the conditions for necessary implication. The author follows the order of the objections, each introduced with a response.

In [أ], the author first establishes that the middle term is 'united' (muttaḥid) in the sense that it is subsumed under both the minor and major premises. The middle term (e.g., 'J D') is contained in the minor and also appears in the major, ensuring the link. He refutes a doubt that separation (iftirāq) between the middle and the minor is impossible; rather, separation can occur if the union is not necessary, but the implication from both sides must hold on all assumptions.

In [ب], the objection is that if the major term is not entailed when the middle is realized without the minor, then it cannot be entailed by the minor. The author responds by noting that the major could still be entailed by the minor, especially if the middle is more general than the minor. He argues that it is impossible for the major to not be entailed by either the minor or the middle in reality; thus, the entailment still holds.

In [ت], the doubt is that separation between the two sides negates perpetual implication. The author clarifies that perpetual implication (al-luzūm al-dā'ima) means the implication holds under all assumptions, including the assumption of separation. Even if separation is assumed, the consequent must still follow from the antecedent when the antecedent is realized.

In [ث], the famous doubt (al-shubha al-mashhūra) is addressed. The example given is a conditional syllogism: 'Whenever two is an odd number; if it is a number, it is even.' The objection says the major premise 'if it is a number, it is even' is false when the number is odd. The author refutes this by noting that 'two' essentially has evenness; regardless of being assumed odd, it remains two and therefore even. This is similar to saying that if a thing is a revealed book (like the Qur'an), it is essentially from God, even if we assume it is not revealed. The author also mentions a second mood that is not productive because the major premise is not universally true; an example is 'human is always a breather and an inhaler', but these are not essentially linked.

The key principle here is that for a conditional syllogism to be valid, the major premise must be a necessary implication (luzūm) that holds in all circumstances, grounded in the essence of the subject.

شبهة
ملزوم
لازم

Checkpoint
In the third objection, what does the author say about perpetual implication?
A) It requires that separation between terms is impossible.
B) It holds under all assumptions, including separation.
C) It only holds if the terms are coextensive.
D) It is not relevant to conditional syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
Perpetual implication holds even under assumptions of separation, as long as the implication is grounded in the essence of the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional statement: 'If the Quran is the word of God, then it is miraculous.' Suppose someone objects that it is possible for a book to be miraculous without being the word of God (separation). Using the author's principle, explain why the implication still holds.

The Famous Doubt: Essential vs. Accidental Implication
Author refutes four criticisms regarding the unity of the middle term in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental implication in conditional syllogisms and identify when a major premise is truly necessary.

Core Idea
A valid conditional syllogism requires the major premise to be an essential implication grounded in the essence of the subject.

The fourth objection presents a famous doubt (al-shubha al-mashhūra): if we combine the premises 'Whenever two is odd' and 'If it is a number, it is even', we would conclude 'If it is odd, it is even', which is false. The author argues that this only seems false because we assume oddness prevents evenness, but for 'two' evenness is essential. He also criticizes a second mood where the major premise is not universally true.

Guided Path
1
Parse the example
The syllogism: (1) Whenever two is odd (minor). (2) If it is a number, it is even (major). Conclusion: If it is odd, it is even. The objector says the major is false because odd numbers are not even.
2
Understand the author's rebuttal
He says evenness is essential to two (bi-ḥasb al-dhāt). Assuming two is odd does not negate its being two; therefore, it remains even. So the major premise is true for the subject 'two', even if oddness is assumed.
3
Distinguish essential from accidental
Essential implication follows from the essence (dhāt). Accidental is contingent on external factors. For a conditional syllogism to be valid, the major premise must be essential.
4
Apply to Islamic science
For example, 'If the Prophet (peace be upon him) said something, it is revelation.' This is essential because prophethood entails truthfulness. If we assume the Prophet said something false, that contradiction does not undermine the essential implication.

Reducing Second Figure Conditional Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author describes the productive moods of the second figure conditional syllogism and shows reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a second figure conditional syllogism to the first figure using contraposition and validate it by proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
The second figure conditional syllogism is reducible to the first figure by contraposition of the major premise.

The author explains that the productive moods of the second figure conditional syllogism follow the same conditions as before. Focusing on the first mood, he shows how it can be reduced to the first figure via contraposition (ʿaks al-kubrā) or proven by contradiction (khalf). This lesson will walk you through these logical techniques, which are essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Recognize the first mood of the second figure: two conditional premises with a common consequent. The major premise is a universal conditional, the minor is a negative universal conditional.
The common consequent here is 'J D'.
2
Apply contraposition
Contraposit the major premise: 'If AB then JD' becomes 'If not JD then not AB'. This transforms the syllogism into the first figure where the middle term becomes the antecedent of the major and consequent of the minor (after adjusting).
Contraposition preserves truth and is a standard reduction technique.
3
Verify by contradiction (khalf)
Assume the conclusion is false, so its contradictory 'If AB then HZ' is true. Conjoin this with the major premise to derive 'If AB then JD' (already given) and 'If AB then HZ', leading to 'If AB then HZ and JD'. Then using the minor premise (which denies 'If HZ then JD'), derive a contradiction, confirming the original conclusion.
This method is commonly used in kalām arguments.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Observe that this pattern appears in fiqh reasoning, e.g., when determining whether a new substance is forbidden based on shared properties.
The second figure conditional syllogism can test for exclusivity of conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الثاني فالضروب المنتجة فيه كما مرَّ ، والشرائط كذلك. أما الضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز ف ج د ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لما مر من الطروق في القياسات الحملية؛ فإنه يرتد إلى الشكل الأول بعكس كبراها فيتبين به، وبالخلف أيضًا كما يقال لو كذب قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز». فنضمه إلى الكبرى
As for the second figure, the productive moods in it are as previously mentioned, and the conditions likewise. As for the first mood—which is our statement: 'Whenever AB, then J D', and it is not at all that if H Z then J D'—it yields our statement: 'It is not at all that if AB then H Z' due to the methods that have passed in categorical syllogisms; for it reduces to the first figure by contraposition of its major premise, and thus it becomes clear. And also by reductio, as it is said: If our statement 'It is not at all that if AB then H Z' were false, then its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'It may be that if AB then H Z'. So we conjoin it to the major premise...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage explains the second figure of conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī) and specifically the first mood of that figure. The author states that the productive moods and conditions are as previously discussed. The first mood is: 'Whenever AB, then J D' (major premise in the form of a universal conditional) and 'It is not at all that if H Z, then J D' (negative universal conditional as minor premise). The conclusion is: 'It is not at all that if AB, then H Z'. The author then notes that this mood can be reduced to the first figure by contraposition (ʿaks) of the major premise. In categorical syllogisms, contraposition involves interchanging subject and predicate with negation; here it means converting the conditional into its contrapositive. Alternatively, a proof by contradiction (khalf) is given: assume the conclusion is false, so its contradictory 'It may be that if AB, then H Z' is true; conjoin this with the original major premise to derive a contradiction. The text ends abruptly at 'fa naḍummuhu ilā al-kubrā' (so we conjoin it to the major premise), but the argument would conclude that the assumption leads to an impossibility, thus proving the original conclusion. This is similar to how a conditional syllogism in Islamic legal reasoning might be used: e.g., 'If it is wine, it is forbidden; if it is not nabidh, it is not forbidden'—the second figure would test for counterexamples.

The Fourth Figure Conditional Syllogism and Necessity in Conditionals
Author defends the validity of the fourth figure conditional syllogism against an objection about necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the necessity (luzum) of a conditional premise in a fourth-figure conditional syllogism and identify essential properties that ground necessity.

Core Idea
A conditional premise is necessary when the consequent follows from the essence of the antecedent.

This passage tackles a famous objection to the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms. The author argues that the syllogism is valid because the major premise is necessary—the connection between being a number and being even is essential to the nature of two. Understanding this helps you see how logical validity depends on the intrinsic properties of the subject, which is crucial for crafting sound arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism structure
Find the two conditional premises: the minor 'whenever two is an odd number' and the major 'if it is a number then it is even'. Note that the minor is assumed true for the sake of argument.
The conclusion 'if it is odd then it is even' follows from combining these premises.
2
Locate the objection
An objector claims the major premise is not necessary, because 'if it is a number' does not always imply 'it is even'—for example, when it is odd. Recognize that this challenges the conditional's luzumiyya status.
3
Understand the rebuttal: essential property
The author responds that evenness is essential (bi-'l-dhat) to two. The assumption of being a number does not remove its identity as two, so it always implies evenness. This establishes necessity.
The key is that the subject ('two') retains its essence under any assumption.
4
Apply to Islamic argumentation
Evaluate a conditional from fiqh or kalam: is the connection essential or arbitrary? For example, 'if wine is intoxicating, it is forbidden'—the prohibition is essential to intoxication, so the conditional is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الرابع وهو الشبهة المشهورة فنقول قد مرَّ من قبل أن مثل هذا القياس ينتج فإن المحقق لهذه القضية وهي كلَّما كانت الاثنان فردًا عددًا إذا وضعها على أنها حق وكان حقا في نفسه أنه إذا كان عددًا كان زوجا يلزم ذلك المحقق أنه إذا كان فردًا كان زوجا لما أنه يجمع بين هاتين المقدمتين؛ ويُسلم أن كل واحدة منهما حق، والقياس ينتج بحسب التسليم على ما عرف ومنهم من أجاب عنه وقال إن الكبرى في حيز المنع إذا كانت لزومية» وذلك لأنه لا يلزم من فرض كونه عددًا في جميع الأحوال أن يكون زوجا؛ وقد كان من جملة تلك الأحوال أن يكون فردًا. غير أنه فاسد لما أن الزوجية للاثنين بحسب الذات، وفرض كونه عددًا لا يخرجه عن كونه اثنين بل فرض كونه فردًا لا يخرجه؛ فكان اثنين إذن كلما كان عددًا، وكان زوجًا كذلك وإلا لا يكون اثنين في جميع الأحوال؛ وذلك لا يمكن؛ إذ الكلام في الاثنين.
As for the fourth, which is the famous problematic case, we say: it has previously passed that such a syllogism produces a conclusion. For whoever verifies this proposition, namely 'whenever two is an odd number' if he posits it as true, and it is true in itself that 'if it is a number then it is even', then it follows for that verifier that 'if it is odd then it is even', because he combines these two premises; and each one of them is granted as true, and the syllogism produces according to the granting as was known. And among them is one who answered and said that the major premise is subject to objection if it is conditional (luzumiyya), because it does not follow from assuming it is number in all states that it is even; and among those states is that it is odd. However, this is invalid because evenness belongs to two by essence, and the assumption of its being a number does not remove it from being two, rather the assumption of its being odd does not remove that; so it is two whenever it is a number, and it is even likewise, otherwise it would not be two in all states; and that is not possible, for the discussion is about two.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms, identified as the 'famous problematic case'. The author begins by affirming that such a syllogism is productive, using an example: the minor premise 'whenever two is an odd number' (a false but granted proposition) and the major premise 'if it is a number then it is even' (a true proposition). From these, the conclusion 'if it is odd then it is even' follows. An objector then claims the major premise is not a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) because 'if it is a number' does not always imply 'it is even' in all states, such as when it is odd. The author refutes this by noting that evenness is essential to the nature of two; the assumption of its being a number does not change its identity as two, so whenever it is a number (i.e., two), it is necessarily even. Therefore, the major premise is necessary and the syllogism is valid.

Second Figure Conditional Syllogism: The Four Productive Moods
Author enumerates the four moods of the second figure conditional syllogism with their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four productive moods of the second figure conditional syllogism and validate them using reduction to the first figure or reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The second figure yields negative conclusions by contraposition or reductio.

In conditional logic, the second figure has four moods that always produce negative or possibly negative conclusions. The author lists them and shows how they are proved by reducing to the first figure or by reductio. Mastering these moods equips you to handle conditional arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology where implications are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. In conditional form, the middle term is the consequent of both conditionals.
2
Recognize the first mood
From 'whenever A then B' and 'never H then B', we conclude 'never A then H'. This is a universal negative conclusion.
3
Understand the proof
The author provides two proofs: conversion (contraposition) of the major premise to reduce to the first figure, and reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the remaining moods
The second mood has 'never A then B' and 'always H then B' giving 'never A then H'. The third and fourth moods involve 'maybe' premises and yield 'maybe not' conclusions.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
For instance, in usul al-fiqh: 'If the evidence is qat'i then the ruling is certain' and 'It is never the case that if the evidence is zanni then the ruling is certain' yields 'It is never the case that if the evidence is qat'i then it is zanni' (i.e., they are incompatible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الثاني فالضروب المنتجة فيه كما مرَّ ، والشرائط كذلك. أما الضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز ف ج د ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ هز» لما مر من الطروق في القياسات الحملية؛ فإنه يرتد إلى الشكل الأول بعكس كبراها فيتبين به، وبالخلف أيضًا كما يقال لو كذب قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز». فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» ، وليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فـ «ج د» فقد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ ج د ؛ وقد كان كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، هذا خلف. والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ ج د»، وكلَّما كان «هز» فــ اج دا ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ هز» لما مر . والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (ج) دا ، وليس البتة إذا كان هز» فـ اج دا فقد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز» لما مر . والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ (ج) دا ، وكلَّما كان هـز فـ (ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ ه ز لما مر .
As for the second figure, the productive moods in it are as mentioned, and the conditions likewise. As for the first mood: we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D, and it is absolutely not the case that when H is Z then C is D' produces 'It is absolutely not the case that when A is B then H is Z' due to the methods mentioned in categorical syllogisms; for it reduces to the first figure by converting its major premise, and also by reductio, as is said: if our statement 'It is absolutely not the case that when A is B then H is Z' were false, its contradictory would be true, namely 'It may be that when A is B then H is Z'. We conjoin it with the major premise and say: 'It may be that when A is B then H is Z, and it is absolutely not the case that when H is Z then C is D; therefore it may be that when A is B then C is D.' But it was the case that whenever A is B then C is D; this is a contradiction. The second mood: 'It is absolutely not the case that when A is B then C is D, and whenever H is Z then C is D' produces 'It is absolutely not the case that when A is B then H is Z' as per previous. The third mood: 'It may be that when A is B then C is D, and it is absolutely not the case that when H is Z then C is D' produces 'It may not be that when A is B then H is Z' as per previous. The fourth mood: 'It may not be that when A is B then C is D, and whenever H is Z then C is D' produces 'It may not be that when A is B then H is Z' as per previous.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage lists and explains the four productive moods of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author first notes that the conditions are as previously established. The first mood: from 'Whenever A implies B' (i.e., whenever A is true, B is true) and 'It is absolutely not the case that H implies B' (i.e., H never implies B), we conclude 'It is absolutely not the case that A implies H'. The proof is given: it reduces to the first figure by converting the major premise (contraposition), and also by reductio ad absurdum—assuming the contrary leads to a contradiction. The second mood: from 'A does not imply B' and 'H always implies B', we conclude 'A does not imply H'. The third and fourth moods involve possible conclusions, indicating negation of implication under certain conditions. These moods are validated by reference to the earlier methods used in categorical syllogisms.

الضرب
الخلف
ليس البتة
قد يكون

Key Takeaway
The second figure conditional syllogism always yields a negative or possibly negative conclusion, proven by reduction to the first figure or reductio.

Three Objections Against the First Mood of the Second Figure Conditional Syllogism
Author raises three objections challenging the validity of the first mood of the second figure conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the three objections raised against the first mood of the second figure conditional syllogism in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The first mood of the second figure faces three objections: unity of the middle term, conversion failure, and generality mismatch.

This passage presents three objections that challenge the validity of the first mood of the second figure conditional syllogism. These objections probe fundamental issues: whether the middle term is truly unified, whether the conclusion can be converted without contradiction, and whether the conclusion is more general than the premises, thereby negating the need for the premises. We will walk through each objection, analyze its logical structure, and understand how they question the necessity of the inference.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the first mood of the second figure conditional syllogism
The first mood of the second figure consists of two conditional premises with the same consequent. The minor premise has the form 'If A then B', and the major premise has the form 'If not A then B'? Actually, the second figure conditional has the middle term as the consequent, not antecedent. But here the objections refer to: minor: If C then D; major: If H then not D. The middle term is D/not D. Understand this structure before analyzing objections.
The second figure conditional syllogism is defined by the middle term being the consequent in both premises.
2
Analyze the first objection: unity of the middle term
The objector argues that the syllogism only produces a conclusion if the middle term is unified (the same in both premises). But in the first mood, the minor has a positive consequent (D) while the major has a negative consequent (not D). Thus, the middle term is not truly unified, so the syllogism fails. Consider whether 'D' and 'not D' can be considered the same middle term.
The author responds that absolute propositions are not opposites; the objection assumes a strict unity that may not be required.
3
Dissect the second objection: conversion and contradiction
The objector claims the conclusion does not necessarily follow. He argues: if it did follow, the conclusion would have to be convertible (its converse always true). But the absolute proposition is not convertible (its converse can be false). If we assume convertibility, then both the conclusion and its converse would be true, leading to a contradiction. Therefore, the conclusion does not follow.
This objection uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming validity leads to a contradiction. Examine the conversion properties of conditional propositions.
4
Examine the third objection: generality and independence
The objector claims the conclusion is more general (a'amm) than the premises. If so, the conclusion could be true from other reasons, so it does not depend on these premises. This would mean the syllogism is not a valid demonstration, as the conclusion could be known independently.
The idea of 'a'amm' relates to logical hierarchy; a more general proposition can be true without a specific premise.
5
Evaluate the collective force of the objections
Consider whether these objections actually refute the first mood or merely highlight subtle conditions that must be met. The objections rely on specific assumptions about unity, conversion, and generality. Assess their validity from the perspective of classical Islamic logic.
The author may respond to these objections in the subsequent text, but here we focus on understanding the objections themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يورد عليها من الشبه أيضًا كما في الضرب الأول؛ وذلك بوجوه: [1] منها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا: وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه الا يكون متحدا إلا وأن يكون (ج) د» في / الصغرى في مقابلة ليس جدا في الكبرى؛ وليس كذلك فإن المطلق لا يكون في مقابلة المطلق. [ب] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج فإنه لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة؛ وذلك لأنه إذا لزم فإما أن تكون النتيجة منعكسة، وذلك لا يمكن لما مر من قبل أن المطلقة تكون (۱) منعكسة أو لا تكون منعكسة، وذلك لا يمكن أيضًا فإنه يمكن أن يصدق نقيض ذلك العكس وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فـ «أ ب فيصدق عكسه كذلك وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز»، وإنه مما ينا في الصدق . [ت] ومنها أن يقال صدق النتيجة في هذه الصورة أعم من صدق المقدمتين لما أنه يلزم من الغير كما يلزم منه . ولو كان أعم منه فلا يفتقر إليه في التحقق ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يفتقر ذلك إلى هذا أيضًا وإلا لكان الافتقار في أحد الجانبين منهما دون الآخر وذلك لا يمكن فإن الافتراق منهما في التحقق
And objections are raised against it (the first mood) also as in the first mood; and that with aspects: [1] Among them is that it is said: it does not produce except if the middle term is unified; but that is subject to objection because it would not be unified except if (C D) in the minor is in contrast to 'not C D' in the major; and that is not the case, for the absolute is not in contrast to the absolute. [b] Among them is that it is said: it does not produce, for from the truth of these two premises the truth of the conclusion does not follow; because if it follows, then either the conclusion is convertible, and that is not possible as previously mentioned that the absolute proposition is convertible or not convertible, and that is also not possible, for the contradictory of that converse could be true, namely 'it may be that when H is Z then A is B' and its converse is also true, namely 'it may be that when A is B then H is Z', and that is something that conflicts with truth. [t] Among them is that it is said: the truth of the conclusion in this form is more general than the truth of the premises, because it follows from something else as it follows from it. And if it is more general than it, then it does not depend on it for verification; and then it follows that it does not depend on this either, otherwise the dependency would be on one of the two sides without the other, and that is not possible, for the separation of the two in verification...
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the first mood of the second figure conditional syllogism. The first objection concerns the unity of the middle term: the objector claims the syllogism works only if the middle term is the same in both premises, but in the first mood, the minor has a positive predicate (C implies D) while the major has a negative (H does not imply D), so they are not truly unified. The author responds (likely in following text) that this is invalid because absolute propositions are not opposites. The second objection argues that the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises; if it did, the conclusion would be convertible, but its converse could be false, leading to contradiction. The third objection claims the conclusion is more general than the premises, so it could be true independently, negating the need for the premises. The objections highlight deep logical issues regarding necessity, conversion, and generality.

Key Takeaway

Formalize the second objection as a conditional argument (if the conclusion follows, then it is convertible; but it is not convertible; therefore it does not follow) and verify its validity using modus tollens.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Second, Third, and Fourth Moods of Conditional Syllogisms
Enumeration of three conditional syllogistic moods with premises and conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second, third, and fourth moods of conditional syllogisms and state their premises and conclusions.

Core Idea
Three moods yield universal negative, particular negative, and particular negative conclusions.

In this passage, the author enumerates three more moods of conditional syllogisms beyond the first. Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its two conditional premises. Understanding these moods allows you to construct valid conditional arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in each mood
For each mood, locate the two conditional statements (the major and minor premises). They appear after 'قولنا' in the text.
The first premise is often marked by the beginning of the mood description.
2
Determine quantity and quality of each premise
Look for key phrases: 'ليس البتة' indicates universal negative, 'كلما' indicates universal positive, 'قد يكون' indicates particular positive, 'قد لا يكون' indicates particular negative.
These correspond to the four types of conditionals studied previously.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion follows the words 'ينتج' (yields) or 'فقد' (thus it may). Note the form: it is always a conditional statement with the same relation between the antecedent and consequent.
The conclusion's quality and quantity are determined by the premises.
4
Summarize the pattern for each mood
Mood 2: Universal negative major, universal positive minor → universal negative conclusion. Mood 3: Particular positive major, universal negative minor → particular negative conclusion. Mood 4: Particular negative major, universal positive minor → particular negative conclusion.
Memorize these patterns for quick identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان «هز» فـ «ج د» ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لما مر. والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فـ «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لما مر. والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان «هز» فـ «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» لما مر.
The second mood: we say 'Absolutely not if A-B then J-D, and whenever H-Z then J-D' yields 'Absolutely not if A-B then H-Z' due to what has passed. The third mood: we say 'It may be that if A-B then J-D, and absolutely not if H-Z then J-D' yields 'It may be that not if A-B then H-Z' due to what has passed. The fourth mood: we say 'It may be that not if A-B then J-D, and whenever H-Z then J-D' yields 'It may be that not if A-B then H-Z' due to what has passed.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage enumerates three moods of conditional syllogisms, the second, third, and fourth. The author states each mood in the form 'our saying ... then it results ... due to what has passed'. The second mood has a universal negative major premise (absolutely not if A-B then J-D) and a universal positive minor premise (whenever H-Z then J-D), and yields a universal negative conclusion. The third mood has a particular positive major and a universal negative minor, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The fourth mood has a particular negative major and a universal positive minor, also yielding a particular negative. The phrase 'due to what has passed' indicates that the proof relies on earlier established principles, likely the reduction to the first mood or similar. This enumeration is typical in the study of conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
الضرب
ليس البتة
قد يكون
قد لا يكون

Checkpoint

C) Particular positive and universal negative
D) Particular negative and universal positive

Key Takeaway
The second, third, and fourth moods of conditional syllogisms are distinguished by the quantity and quality of their premises, yielding specific conclusion forms.

Structure of the First Mood of the Third Figure of Conditional Syllogism
Describes the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogism and state its conclusion.

Core Idea
The third figure's first mood links two consequents through a shared antecedent.

In conditional syllogisms, the arrangement of antecedent and consequent across premises determines the figure. The third figure is characterized by the same antecedent appearing in both premises. This lesson examines the first mood of that figure, which produces a particular conditional conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The passage states two universal conditional premises: 'Whenever A-B then J-D' and 'Whenever A-B then H-Z'. Note that both share the same antecedent 'A-B'.
The letters are variables representing propositions.
2
Recognize the figure
Since the common term (the antecedent 'A-B') is the subject in both premises, this is the third figure. In categorical syllogisms, the third figure has the middle term as subject; here it's adapted for conditionals.
3
Determine the conclusion
The author states that the conclusion is 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z'. This is a particular conditional (muqaddara juz'iyya).
The conclusion is not universal because conversion of the minor only yields a particular conditional.
4
Verify the mood
This is the first mood of the third figure. It takes the form: If A then B, and if A then C; therefore, it may be that if B then C.
Other moods would have different quantifiers or negations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما في الشكل الثالث فالضروب فيه كما مر، والشرائط كذلك؛ والضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان «أب» فـ «هز» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» بالرد إلى الأول؛ وذلك بعكس صغراه، أو بالخلف مثلا كما يقال لو كذب قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز». فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» فليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز»; وقد صدق قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ ...
[3] As for the third figure, the moods are as have passed, and the conditions likewise; the first mood is our saying 'Whenever A-B then J-D, and whenever A-B then H-Z' yields 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z' by reduction to the first figure; that is by converting its minor premise, or by reductio, for example, as is said: if our statement 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z' were false, then its contradictory would be true, namely our statement 'Absolutely not if J-D then H-Z'. Then we combine it with the minor and say: 'Whenever A-B then J-D, and absolutely not if J-D then H-Z, so absolutely not if A-B then H-Z'; and our statement 'Whenever A-B then ...' is true.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage explains the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogism. The premises are two universal conditional propositions sharing the same antecedent (A-B). The conclusion is a particular conditional: 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z.' The author explains that this mood can be reduced to the first figure in two ways: first, by converting the minor premise (i.e., from 'Whenever A-B then H-Z' to 'It may be that if H-Z then A-B'), then with the other premise forming a first figure syllogism. Second, by reductio (al-khalf): assume the conclusion is false, i.e., 'Absolutely not if J-D then H-Z', combine with the minor premise to derive 'Absolutely not if A-B then H-Z', which contradicts the given premise 'Whenever A-B then H-Z', thus proving the conclusion. This shows the validity of the mood.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الضرب الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The same antecedent
C) Different antecedents
D) The same conclusion

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the third figure has two universal conditionals with identical antecedents, yielding a particular conditional conclusion linking the consequents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following: 'If one travels, then one may shorten the prayer' and 'If one travels, then one may break the fast'. Using the first mood of the third figure, what conclusion can you draw? Write it in the form 'It may be that if ... then ...'.
